TOO SHARP
BATTLES WITH
THE YAQUIS
Nineteen Mexican Soldiers
Are Killed and Several
Wounded.
FOUR OFFICERS FALL
â¦
Inhabitants of River Towns Who
Were Taken Prisoners by the
Indians Are Released.
â¦
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES. Sept. 24.â Official
reports of two battles between
Mexicans and Yaquis have just
been received here. General
Luis Torres had declared the j
campaign suspended until October,
but the Yaquis were not consult
ed on that point. Lorenzo Torres
j also appears to have made different j
I arrangements.
On September 14 Lorenzo Torres
j crossed the river to Vicam. a town sup
posed to have been captured by Luis |
Torres in August, and found the Indi- |
ans. They attacked his rear guard,
and a running fight ensued. The report
says the Indians were dispersed and
nine killed. The Mexican loss was five
killed and nine wounded, among the
latter Lieutenant Colonel Navarro of j
the Eleventh Battalion, one of the best !
officers on the Mexican side. v -
The report of a battle on the 18th
comes from another source, also offi
cial, and its statements of the number j
killed may be accepted. According to
this report the forces under Colonel
Hernandez (number not stated) en
countered 1000 Indians near Lake Za
quaca, a small lagoon between Torin
and Potam. The Indians retired from
the open field and sought cover In the
thick woods, where the combat raged
for more than two hours, beginning at
6 o'clock in the morning. The report
says the Indians were defeated and dis
persed, leaving on the field ST dead.
The Mexican losses are stated as four- j
teen soldiers- killed and thirty-six
wounded. Major Ruiz of the National
Guard was mortally wounded, and
Julian Esponza. captain of the Yaquis I
â who are friendly to the Mexicans, and j
Tetebins. a lieutenant in the ten years" j
war, were killed. An interesting feat
ure of the reports is the statement that
several inhabitants of river towns who
â were taken prisoner by the Yaquis in
July have been released and have re
turned to headquarters in Torin. Here
tofore the Mexicans have represented
the Yaquis as invariably torturing and
killing all prisoners.
BROUGHT A WRECKED
VESSEL INTO PORT
Engineer of the Boscowitz Saves Her
After She Was Given Up
as Lest.
VICTORIA, R. C. Sept. 24,â Much to
the surprise of shipping men the steamer
Barbara Boscowitz steamed into the
harbor this morning:. When thÂ« steamer
Princess J.oulse, which arrived on Friday,
left the Skeena, the Boscowitz was lying
almost submerged; with the water rac
ing through her hold, stranded on an old
Ice break, r. Her boiler and engines had
phlfted and some of her steam pipes were
broken.
She was patched with canvas and
cement and the water pumped from her.
Then her engineers set out to fix the
Jumbiod up engines and broken pipes.
How they accomplished this is a surprise
to all engineers who lihvp viewed the
pitched up engines, Kngineer McGregor
with characteristic modesty will not talk
of his feat. "I had been reading about
how those fellows fixed up their engines
In Kipling's story 'Between the Devil and
the Deep Sea,' and I thought to myself
that if engines could be fixed under such
circumstances, surely we could get ours
straightened up enough to bring her
down."
This is the second time within a year
that the Boscowitz has been given up as
lest and she ha been saved.

EARTHQUAKE IN ASIA MINOR
CONSTANTINOPLE, Sept. 24.â The district of Adam, in Asia Minor,
was visited by an earthquake on September 20. and according to the
latest advices over 200 persons perished.
The chief cities of Ihe vilayet or province of Adam are Smyrna, its
capital, Maneesa and Aidin. The city of Aidin is about eighty miles
southeast of Smyrna. It is the residence of the Pasha and the focus of
a flourishing trade in grain, carpets, figs, opium and sponges.

At Ocean Grove there are great

Drowned While Swimming.
LOS ANGELES, Sept. 24.â Herman
Bergeroff, the janitor of Turner Hall in
this city, was drowned at Ballona Beach
to-day. He had gone to Ballona for a
day's'outing with his wife and child. He
was a good swimmer and attempted to
swim across the bay, but was unable to
reach the shore, having doubtless been
seized with a cramp. The body has been
recovered.
ITALIANS AT HARBOR VIEW.
Anniversary of Capture of Rome Ob
served With Fitting Ceremo
nial by Latin Residents.
Harbor View Park took on a gala ap
pearance yesterday when the Italian citi
zens of the city congregated there to
celebrate the 29th anniversary of the
capture of Rome. Early in the morning
j the Bersaglierl formed at their armory
I and, preceded by their band, marched to
1 the park, presenting a splendid martial
appearance as they passed from street to
Street on toward thoir destination..
At the park nearly every prominent
member of the Latin colony had congre
gated, and as soon ns the warriors ar
rived mirth was proclaimed king of the
hour and joyousness reigned supreme.
During the daylight hours music,
dances and athletic sports helped to pass
the hours along, and when the lights be
gan to pale their banquet board was
1 spread and wassal followed the more
exacting pleasures of the earlier hours.
Before the fun ceased another day had
almost commenced, and the guests de
parting vowed that many a day would
come and go before another one so
pleasant would make its appearance.
Those who were elected to fill the tem
porary offices of honor yesterday were
J. C. Sala. president of the day; Cavalier
Serra, Italian Consul, master of cere
monies, and G. Fugazi, orator of the
day.

1 art country; thence back to the south I
fork of the Klondike and home. They
prospected on many creeks, but only
colors were found. Owing to the fact
that rimrock . was exposed on the
creeks, prospecting was easy. They are
satisfied there is no . gold between
Hunker and the Porcupine and the |
Rockies. Some rich quartz specimens

THE INJURED IN THE
TRAIN WRECK BETTER
PUEBLO, Colo., Sept. 24.â A1l the in
jured in the wreck of Saturday afternoon
on the Rio Grande Railroad near Canyon
City are doing well at the hospitals here.
Mrs. F. A. Wills of Los Angeles, both
of whose legs were broken, and C. R.
Cook of Millers, Tnd., whose shoulder was
brokenâ these two being the wost seri
ously injuredâ are getting along quite
well, though neither is out of danpr-r.
The passengers who were slightly hurt
will be able to continue their journey in
a few days. The bodies of all the dead
were brought here. Some have been
shipped on instructions from relatives,
while others are awaiting disposition.
Such investigation of the wreck as has
been made thus far places the blame on
the crew of the freight train. The Coro
ner's jury will nut make its findings
known until to-morrow.

A NATIONAL
CIVIC SOCIETY
â  â : Â«
The Plan of the Chicago
Federation.
Â«
Special Dispatch. to "flip Call.
CHICAGO. Sept. 24.â executive com
mittee of the Civic Federation of Chicago
has authorized its president. Franklin H.
Head, to appoint a committee of twenty
five to organize a National Civic Federa
tion. The committee is to be composed of
men representing different sections of the
country and. different phases of social and
economical questions of the day. The
object of such an organization is stated
to be to provide for a thorough discussion
and consideration of questions of national
importance, affecting either the foreign
or domestic policy of the United States;
to aid in .the crystallization of themost en
lightened' public sentiment in tne country
In * respect/ thereto, and to pro
mote necessary legislation in ac
cordance therewith. The leaders of
the civic federation : have contem
plated this move for some time, deem
ing it essential if a national work were
to become anything more than educa
tional. . â¢ ; .-. *
In January; 1898, the Chicago Civic
Federation called a national conference
on primary elections ana municipal re
forms, which was held in New York, and
was attended by representatizes of forty
of the largest cities of the United States.
The Impetus given to primary election
reform resulted in both New York and
Illinois securing laws at the following
sessions of their respective legislatures.
But beyond- this and the- publication and
distribution of proceedings, the Chicago
Federation has found it impracticable to
go. In August. 189S, it called at Saratoga
a national conference on the foreign pol
icy of the United States, which, after two
days of animated debate by the leading
men of all parties, unanimously agreed
upon some broad general principles which
called for further effort to render it of
permanent value to the country. This
work could not be followed by the Chi
cago organization. *
The four days' trust conference recently
held in Chicago was not expected to agree
upon general principles, but it was be
lieved the free discussion by representa
tives Of so many schools of thought would
furnish material and a basis for more
thorough work hereafter. It is to make
it possible to follow up the practical lines
suggested by the conference that the na
tional civic federation is projected. .
DR. JORDAN SAYS THE
WAR IS UNJUSTIFIABLE
A Strong Nation Has No Bight to
Take the Lands of a Weaker
One.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Sept. 24.â
Dr. David Starr Jordan occupied the pul
pit of the University chapel this morning
and delivered a vigorous address, in which
he touched upon the subject of imperial
ism In his usual terse way. Taking as his
text, "It needs be that offenses must
come, but woe to him that doth offend," !
he said:
Inevitableness does" not justify anything. j
What some people have termed an "irresisti- '
ble tendency" in nations or affairs fades when
a man stands before It. The hero is the j
man who takes the inevltableness in hand and
makes the necessary sacrifice. There [a a will
ingness upon the part b{ many to excuse an
hereditary vice, saying: "My rather was a
drunkard: how can I keep sober?" You must
remember that it was not your father's whisky,
but his weakness that you Inherited. Nature
never accepts any excuses, the effect being the
fame as If done with Intention.
Given a fool with money and the world as
we know it. and. it Is a sure prediction that
the fool and his money will soon bo parted.
This, however, does not Justify the business
of the gambler or the swindler.
I know a great nation that said to a little
nation: "Give up your weapons, gold and lands
to me or they will get you Into trouble." Some
argue that there are feeble nations on the
earth that are going to be exploited, so why
should not we exploit them" i believe that
there should be ethics among nations as well
as among men. John Brown truly said from
the scaffold that God Is no respecter of per
sons. He does not care whether a nation be
strong and wealthy with a boasted religion
if it does not obey His laWw. â¢
Through all the ages the slave has had his
revenge on hi:- master In that the effect of his
servitude always worked demoralization upon
the oppressor. Men who Justify existing
things by such phrases as "manifest destiny"
or "irresistible tendency' are generally either
too lazy to look into affairs or too slothful
to care. Such men ore the first to cry. "My
country right or wrong." Nothing can en
dure by which one set of men exist at the
expense of another.
It is the "manifest destiny" of the British
Empire to possess the land and vineyards of
the Dutch farmers in the Transvaal. "Just
bo sure as the British flag Is carried over the
Rand, Just so sure will It prove the disinteg
ration of the empire," wrote Â» celebrated Eng
lish scholar to me. Another Englishmanâ
time a soldierâ says: "Courage like CChambe r
lain's Is cheap enough. The tighter affairs
become, the tighter will he screw his monocle,
and If the people become tired of him he will
retire to his orchards, while I lie a victim of
dumdum bullets."
There Is no excUÃe for assisting the laws of
evolution by crushing the weak, nor putting
a banana . peel under the heel of the lame. -
The basis: of society is Justice and sympathy.
The lion and eagle typify things , In national
life â  that must pass away. Because : the An
glo-Saxon race has produced good men have
I confidence In it, and not because it is great
and strong.

PLANS GREAT WORK
â¦
Says San Francisco Has Outstripped
the East and Is Now the "Front
Yard" of the Nation.
â¦- â â 
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. Sept. 24. â Dr. Benja
min Ide Wheeler, the new president of
the University of' California, is at the
Hoffman House with Mrs. Wheeler and
their little son. He starts to-morrow
for San Francisco to begin the fall
work. The university will have as a
hew feature within the next year a
college of commerce, in which, Profes
sor Wheeler says, the students will be
educated so they may be prepared to
enter the Consular service of the
! United States. He told a reporter last
; night there was no doubt about the
\ rapid development of the Pacific Coast,
| and hereafter the United States would
| be one of the greatest exporting na
i tions, inquiring young men of special
; training to ' compete successfully in
China with the German and English
university men.
In speaking of the aims of the uni
versity, which, as is well known, Mrs.
I Hearst is greatly interested in. Dr.
j Wheeler said:
"Sometimes I am asked the question
if we are not overdoing university
work, with Stanford and. the State
University so close together. Certainly
I not. The 'State University has 2500
I students, and they are coming so fast
we don't know what to do with them.
During the last year it was necessary
to place some of them in tents. Four
temporary buildings are being used for
their accommodation.
"The freshman class this year num
bers 620 already, a gain of more than
ils per cent over last year. This does
| not include the professional schools
! and graduate departments. The ' mi
i crease in the attendance is so rapid it
is becoming a serious problem what we
; are to do with them, as no tuition is
j charged, 'f*he whole Pacific Coast is
j waiting for the State University to
take care of its boys. People here in
the East do not appear to appreciate
' how rapid â¢ the development is out
there. Owing to recent natural devel
opments San Francisco has really be
i come the 'front yard' of the nation in
| stead of the 'back yard.' San Fran
cisco is about the center of our posses
sions. The work of the two universi
j ties does not conflict. Stanford is a
private institution and possesses cer
tain advantages by reason of its being
such., It can do as it likes. The two
institutions work together and are a
great assistance to each other. ' Each
has its own field, and while there is a
healthy rivalry there is no conflict of
interests. Every advance one makes
will be of advantage to the other. Ex
perience has proved that already."

dered him. either in the Harmon gymna

FEVER IN MEXICO.
Death Bate Much Higher Than in
Any Previous Epidemic.
AUSTIN. Sept. 24.â The State Health
Department here to-day received an of
ficial report from Vera Cruz, Mex., stat
ing that during the period from Septem
ber 1 to 9 there were twenty-one cases of
yellow fever in that city, of which ten
resulted fatally. The death rate from
the disease is much higher than in any
previous epidemic.

SAMUEL STEWART
PASSES AWAY
One of the Pioneers of
* the Early Days.
>
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SUISUN, Sept. 24.â gloom was cast
over this community to-day when word >
reached here that Samuel Stewart had
died at his home, near Denverton, early
this morning. Although his death was
not unexpected, it was not thought when
the last report was received last evening
that the. end was so near at hand. His
ailment was heart disease. The deceased
was a prosperous and successful farmer
and well known, not alone in Solano
County, but throughout the State. He
! was a prominent Mason, being a mem-
I ber of Suisun Lodge No. 55, F. and A. M.;
Solano Chapter No. 43, R. A. M ; Naval
Commandery No. 19, Knights Templar,
and the Masonic Veteran Association. He
will be buried in Fairfield Cemetery
Tuesday, the ceremonies to be conducted
by the Suisun lodge. â¢ '
"Samuel Stewart was born in Newark,
Ohio. August 13, 1828. He arrived in Cali
fornia in 1849, first locating in Sacra
! mento. Seven years later he came to
i Solano County, and for forty-three years
I lived where he first located. He was a
1 leader among men: a man of sterling
qualities and highly honored by all who
knew him. His death will be a public
! loss. â¢ *
The death of Mr. Stewart numbers the
1 third in the list of old residents in this
: immediate vicinity within the past four
days. Henry Carpenter of Fairneld. a
resident of forty-five years, died Thurs
day afternoon of cancer of the stomach,
after a lingering illness of many months,
and was buried Saturday. He was 66
years of age. John G. Lewis, a prom
inent .Grand Army man and member of
the Masnnic fraternity, died Saturday and
will be buried under the auspices of the
order to-morrow (Monday). He was in
his sixtieth year and had been a resident
of Suisun twelve years. He leaves a
widow and one son and a daughter, the
! latter Mrs. Frederic Belasco of San Farn-
I cisco. ____ ___________

gations That Suicide Clubs

of Bohemian residents of Chicago was

speches were in Bohemian. Frank B.

LAKE ERIE SWEPT BY
A TERRIFIC STORM
Four Yachts Capsized and Houses
Badly Damaged in North
ern Ohio.
TOLEDO, Sept. 24.â Lake Erie this
afternoon witnessed the worst storm of
the season, the wind blowing at the rate
of seventy miles an hour. Four yachts
were capsized near Maumee Bay and the
crews were rescued with difficulty.
Considerable damage to houses is re
ported in northwestern Ohio.
CHICAGO. Sept. 24.â During a severe
wind and rainstorm this evening the
steam barge Cleveland, laden with 100.000
feet of lumber, sunk in the harbor near
the mouth of the Chicago River.
Captain Henry Davis and a crew of
eleven men were rescued by tugs with
considerable difficulty. The Cleveland
left Frankfort, Mich.. Saturday. She
sprung a leak off Waukegan and. owing
to her water-logged condition, was driven
on a reef while attempting to reach the
inner harbor here. The vessel was built
thirty years ago.

Phebe Hearst.

MANILA, Sept. 24. 11:25 a. m.â
The United States cruiser
Ch.-trieston, the monitor Mon
terey and the gunboats Con
cord and Zaflro, with marines
and bluejackets from the cruiser Balti
more, left Cavite September 19 and, as
already cabled, proceeded to Subig Ray
to destroy an insurgent cannon there.
< 'wing to the bad weather the operation
was postponed until yesterday, when
the warships for three hours bombard
ed the town of Olangapo and the in
tr^nchmetits where the gun was situ
ated.
Men froi'n the Charleston, Concord
and Zaflro were then landed under a
heavy insurgent fire, proceeding to the
cannon, which was utterly destroyed
by gun cotton, and then returned to
the warships. The Americans had one
man wounded during the engagement.
While waiting in Suhisj Bay for better
weather the Americans descried Fili
pino reinforcements moving toward
olangapo. At 6:40 a. m. yesterday the
Monterey began to advance upon the
town, which was about three miles
east of the monitor's anchorage. The
Charleston, Concord and Zafiro fol
lowed.
At 7:25 the Monterey opened fire with
her secondary and main batteries, the
Charleston and Concord joining imme
diately. At 7:30 the Insurgents' cannon
answered, the first shot passing close
to the Monterey's smokestack. The
gun was fired twice only.
The American bombarding then be
came general. At 9:30 the Monterey
advanced to a range of 600 yards, us
ing her main battery. At 11 o'clock
250 men were landed about 800 yards
east of the cannon under a severe Mau
ser fire. The men from the Charleston
were the first to reach the beach, but
the Concord's men were the first at the
gun, which they reached at 11:10. The
cannon was found to be a sixteen-cen
timeter Krupp gun, presumably ob
tained from thf> Spaniards.
Meanwhile the warships continued to
shell the shelving beach on the east
and west side to silence the insurgent
fire upon the sailors from the trenches
skirting the beach.
Gunner Olson exploded fifty rounds
of gun cotton In three discharges in the
cannon, which ha<] .suffered from the
fire from the warships.
The Americans then returned to the
boats, the firing inland being kept up
to pr< tect the embarkation. The Con
cord's men were the last to reach the
chore, and the warships were reached
at 11:50.

launch, armed with a Catling gun. did
> xcellent work on the left of the land
ing party. Captain Meyers of the ma
SACRAMENTO'S CHIEF OF
POLICE PASSES AWAY
SACRAMENTO, Pept. 24.â Chief of Police Thomas Dwyer died at his
Irnme in this city at 7:30 o'clock to-night. While he had been ailing for
peveral months, he was not confined to his bed until two weeks ago. Death
resulted fmm pulmonary and kidney trouble.
Chi^f Dwyer was about 34 years of age, and leaves a widow and two
children, one an infant. He was an excellent officer and had hosts of warm
friends. He was a member of the Klk* and of the Bohemian Club.
Thomas Dwyer enjoyed the distinction of being the youngest man ever
appointed or elected Chief of Police of Sacramento. He was born in Syd
ney, Australia, and came to California at an early age, and resided in Sacra
mento City continuouÃly. He was for fifteen years in the employ of the
Southern Pacific Company in Sacramento.

WASHINGTON, Sept. 24. â Acting
Secretary Allen of the Navy Depart
ment received a cablegram from Rear
Admiral Watson to-day giving a brief
account of the engagement at Sublg
Hay. It is dated the 24th, at Manila,
and says:
Mandora discovered heavy gun mounted
opposite Kalaklan Point. Subig Bay; ex
changed shots with Charleston. Sent
Charleston, Monterey, Concord and Zaflro
with detachment of marines and sailors
from Baltimore to capture and destroy.
Attacked insurgent position 23d; after bom
bardment landing party carried lntreiich
ments, dispossessed enemy and destroyed
one sixteen centimeter Krupp gun. Cas
ualties: William Shepherd, apprentice sec
ond class, Charleston. Bertously wounded;
Charles Haffke, coal passer. Concord,
heat prostration. Insurgent fire heavy
but poorly aimed; enemy loss unknown.
A report mailed.
The Important feature of the engage
ment is the fact which is disclosed that
the insurgents have been able to obtain
a Krupp gun. It was known at the
time of the outbreak of the insurgents
against the Americans that the artil
lery of the insurgents Consisted of a
few obsolete guns captured from the
Spaniards.
Acting Secretary Allen says it is evi
dent that the navy at Manila does not
intend to allow any fort to be erected
by the insurgents which can be reached
by the ships, and he is convinced that
the squadron under Rear Admiral Wat-
Bon will be able to keep the shores
guarded and repeat the lesson of yes
terday if the insurgents are found to
be operating on the coast.

The committee on distribution of tick

were applications for more than 100,000.

sembly and the school children's
stands. These tickets will be at the
j disposal of the municipal legislators

cation.

' stands for all the expenditures it has
i authorized it will have spent more than
i $45,000 in excess of the first appropria

; more than it has in sight even if the
j Municipal Assembly votes to give it
the additional $25,000 that is to be asked j
for at Tuesday's meeting of the Alder
men and Council.
For the seventy-odd thousand ex
pended the committee has furnished
about 20,000 seats to the ticket commit-
I teeâ 4ooo of the City Hall stands, 6000
i for the school children, 10,000 for the
I municipal Assembly â 25,000 for the
j veterans of the Grand Army of the Re-
I publicâ a total of about 45,000 seats, for
I about $74,000.
Work on the big Dewey triumphal
arch is rapidly being pushed, and the
arch will be in proper condition Â» in
plenty of time. To-day while some
, workmen were raising the figure of a
i sailor to the top of a pedestal a Broad
way car ran against one of the guy
ropes of the derrick, and derrick, statue
and workmen fell over on top of the
car. There was a slight panic among
the thousands of people watching the
structure from the sidewalks, but the
police finally restored order. No one
j was hurt, and the figure of the sailor
was but slightly damaged.

HIGHLANDERS WILL NOT
BE IN THE PROCESSION
TORONTO, Ont., Sept. 24.â A special
from Ottawa to the Mail and Kmpire
says:
The Forty-eighth Highlanders of
Toronto will not be granted permission to
visit New York to take part in the recep
tion to Admiral Dewey. The Militia De
partment has not received the communi
cation from the district commanding of
ficer at Toronto embodying the request of
Lieutenant Colonel Crosby and his of
ficers for permission to make the trip,
but when it comes, an answer in the
negative will be returned.
The view held here is that Admiral
Dewey is to be the central figure in the
New York celebration, yet, after all, the
demonstration really will be a glorifica
tion of the United States victories in the
Spanish war. Under the circumstances,
therefore, it is considered very impolitic
for a British regiment to participate. The
pipe band of the Royal Scots of Montreal
has also been refused permission to take
part in the proposed demonstration.
NATIVES SCUTTLE
GUNBOAT URDANETA
MANILA. Sept. 25.â The gunboat Urda
neta. belonging to the mosquito fleet
which has been patrolling the shore on
the north of Manila Bay, has not been
heard from for ten days. Her coal supply
must by this time necessarily be exhaust
ed, and search for her was made. Natives
who were interrogated by lieutenant
Franklin, in command of the gunboat La
guna rie Bai, told that officer the Urda
neta bombarded the town of Orani yes
terday. The crew landed to take posses
sion, whereupon they were seized by a
large body of natives, who had set a trap
for them. The prisoners were removed to
Porac and the gunboat looted and scut
tled. The gunboats Petrel, Callao and
Basco are now searching for the sunken
vessel.

Accident at Keswick.
REDDING Sept. 24.â Joseph Burton,
foreman of a bridge crew for the Moun
tain Copper Company at tne Keswick
smelters, was repairing a roof this after
noon when the galvanized roof, rusted

Special Dispatch to The Call. ..
CAT.I. HEADQUARTERS. WELLING
TON-HOTEL. WASHINGTON, Sept 24.â
Vice President Hobarfs poor health, it Is
now generally believed, will make it im
possible for him to accept the renomlna
tion next year. < â¢:â â 
The President and Republican leaders
generally favor his nomination for the
second place on the ticket if he believes
he is well enough to accept a second time.
Recent reports, however, give little
ground for^ the hope that he will be able
to do so. In that event it is believed the
nomination will go to New York. In view,
of Governor Roosevelt's determination
not to accept it, Secretary of War Root
will probably be named.
The success in the Philippines, which is
expected this winter, will bring Secretary !
Root's administration of the War Depart- j
ment into notice and will make him a '
strong candidate. If the campaign should !
be such, however, as not to reflect credit
on the War Department, some one else
will htÂ»Ve to be obtained.
President McKinley may go into the
campaign for re-election not only with a
new candidate on the ticket with him
but with a new manager of the campaign.
It is well known that Senator Hanna is
not in good health. It is said by one of
his close political friends In Ohio that he
docs not intend to undertake the manage
men:, of another Presidential campaign.
He will not resign from the chairman
ship of the committee. He will continue
i.n that office until the convention next
year. The committee will then be re
organised, as it is every four years, and
Senator Hanna will ask that some one
else be put in charge of the campaign a3
chairman. It is too early now to predict
who will be selected, as the choice will be
made by the new committee, which will
be elected by the convention.

BANK ROBBER AND
MURDERER IS PARDONED
Daniel F. Dougherty, Who Killed Hia
Accomplice in a Quarrel Over
the Spoils, Free.
NEW YORK. Sept. 24.â A cable,n-am re
ceived in tliis city from Manchester, Eng
land, announces the release from prison
there on Friday of Daniel F. Dougherty,
alias Big Doc, the notorious American
bank robber and murderer. The ex-con
vict is now probably on his way to the
Tutted States.
In a quarrel, most likely over the di
vision of plunder, in England, on Decem
ber 1, 1887, Dougherty killed an American
confederate named Graham. He was con
victed of murder and sentenced to life
Imprisonment at Manchester. His pardon
was secured on account of the prisoner's
ill health. Dougherty was arrested In 1868
for bank robbery and was sentenced to
fifteen years' Imprisonment at Charles
town. Mass. Tie served his full term and
was released in 1883.

The picturesque ritual, interspersed
with frequent music by a local quar
tet, was lengthy. The services closed
with the singing: of the Russian na
tional hymn, after which the couple
received congratulations while the or
chestra discoursed music. The cere
mony consumed one hour, and it was
nearly midnight when the guests de
parted.
The betrothal began with the Great
Ectenia, followed by prayers and spe
cial petitions for the salvation of the
betrothed couple, for the blessing of
their offspring and peaceful mutual af
fection. The priest then took the rings
he had received from the bride and
groom. With the gold one, represent
ing the domination of the groom,
three crosses above the groom's head
were made, and then it was Slipped
upon the fourth finger of the groom's
hand. Similar details were gone
through with the sliver ring and the
bride. After this Honire Palmer,
proxy for Grand Duke Cyril of Russia,
acting as best man, or sponsor, for
th.- Prince, stepped forward and trans
posed the rings three times, finally
leaving the gold ring on the bride's
finger and the silver one on the
groom's, these rings being given in
token of the lifelong union into which
they were r-iHcring.
The exchange by the sponsor in
dicated the consent of the family. The
rite closed by the prayer that the
Lord may establish these espousals in
the faith, in harmony, truth and love,
and may bless from heaven the putting
on of rings.
Next came the rite of a marriage.
The couple approached toward the
lecturn, still holding the lighted can
dles. As they proceeded thÂ« quartet
sang Psalm XVI. Halting upon a rug,
the priest inquired of them if they had
spontaheons wishes and firm intention
to contract the conjugal union with
each other, and whether they had
promised to contract the union with
any one else. These questions properly
answered, the priest asked a blessing
upon the couple. The priest then
crowned the Prince and then the
Princess.
A cup of wine was brought and
blessed. The couple drank from it
alternately three times each. The
priest then took them by the hands
and led them three times around the
lecturn, the best man and attendants
following, holding the crowns above
the heads of the newly wedded pair.

INDIAN
MURDERER IS
SURROUNDED
Officers Hot on the Trail of
Joe Pete, Who Killed
Willie Danberg.
HIDING INMOUNTAINS
â¦ â
Washoe Criminal Is Shut Off Prom
Sources of Food and Must Starve
or Submit to Arrest.
â¢
Special Dispatch to The CaH.
ANGELS CAMP, Sept. 24.â Joe Pete,
the Washoe Indian who shot and killed
Willie Danberg at Gardnerville, Nev.,
on the Bth inst., will probably be cap
tured within a few days. After com
mitting the atrocious deed he made his
escape to the vicinity of Gardners,
where he had relatives. Since that time
he has been seen, and has probably
been kept supplied with provisions by
his own people. Had it not been that a
party from Nevada came over to Gard
ners and heralded their mission in the
camp of the Indian's relatives he would
have probably now been in the hands
of the law.
Last Thursday a Mrs. Sapp was driv
ing cattle, when an Indian was seen to
cross the road and disappear in the
brush, but not until she had recognized
him as Pete. He was also seen at a
cattle camp later. He is now definitely
located, and lives within a radius of
seven miles. When the officers first
made their appearance among the
Washoes at Gardners two sisters of
Joe Pete commenced to cry and lament
the fact that the outlaw would be
taken
The officers from this place have now
! determined upon a plan by which the
murderer can be taken at the proper
time. The members of the tribe who
have spent the summer around Gard
ners are now being taken to the upper
| mountains, where they came from, and
' where Pete cannot go without being
taken. This will leave him to shift
for himself, and as soon as the cold
weather comes will drive him to some
camp for provisions. He is completely
â  surrounded, and cannot get out of the
j country. ____________

CALIFORNIA WELCOMES
THE MINERS` GUESTS
VISIT OF AMERICAN INSTITUTE
OF MINING ENGINEERS OF GREAT
IMPORTANCE TO CALIFORNIA
THE California meeting of the American Institute of Mining Engin
eers which will begin in San Francisco to-day, lasting three days, and
attended and followed by much hospitality throughout the State,
will bo composed of an uncertain percentage of about 3000 of the
most eminent and progressive leaders of the mining industry, which
cuts po great and so growing a figure in California's prosperity and indus
trial future. The institute is the largest technical society in the United
States, and it has from Its organization twenty-eight years ago been the
most progressive and successful one. On the special train which brings a
compact lOastern party to the city to-day are 1 ut comparatively few of the
members, but forty or so thus coming together will be supplemented by a
large number of members who will gather individually from all over the
Pacific Coast and elsewhere. The significance of the meeting to California
Is not to be measured by the number of members in attendance. It is not
* popular gathering, but those who come are leaders of a great industry,
with quick perceptions and a wish to know of California's mineral resources
and its human good fellowship, as well of the famed delights which nature
has given it. This is an extremely busy day in the mining and metallurgical
world and many who would have come in a less exacting time have ex
pressed keen regrets at not being able to .attend the most interesting of
seventy-three meetings of the institute.
It is worth more to the State that one man of this stamp who leads
mining capital and the expansion of the industry should learn of Califor
nia's possibilities than that fifty members of a popular society should
hear of what is underground in "California's veined hills and mountains.
The greatest benefit may be a reflex one, resulting from a season uf stimu
lated interest in California's mines and untold mineral resources, from an
awakening to an effort to declare the vast mineral possibilities of the State
and from the hearty extension of hospitality to worthy guests which blesses
him who gives. The irstitute has been a powerful influence in mining prog
resa to which California was much, and the entire State will join the miners
organized in the California Miners' Association in extending a cordial wel
come and California treatment to those who are about to travel hundreds
of miles among our chief mineral regions. For three days beginning to
day the sessions of the meeting will help enliven the Palace Hotel, and
then for nearly three weeks a party of 150 or more will travel about the'
State to see places of the State's resources that have not before attracted
parties of distinguished visitors.

THE SAN FBANCISCO CAIVL, MONDAY, SEPTEMBER 25. 1899.
STORY OF HOW
SAN FRANCISCO
WAS SELECTED
Secretary of State Association
Tells of Efforts Resulting in
Institute's Coming.
E. 11. Benjamin, the indefatigable secre
tary of the California Miners' Asspcla
tion, was the Hist to start the bucci ssful
effort which resulted in the coming to
San Francisco of the American Institute
of Mining Engineers for it* fall meeting
uf 1899. He tells the story as follows:
"During December last 1 was address
ing the California Miners' Association
with R. Gilman J'.rown, manager of the
Standard Consolidated mine of Bodie,
| mainly with reference to the poesibilHi.-s
of organizing a Mono County association.
' Be suggested that it might be a good plan
j to make the association more technical in
its scope, ami in that connection suggest
ed that it would be a splendid idea if the
American Institute of Mining Engineers
could be brought out here. 1 said at
once that I would take It up if It met with
the approval of the association, and on
my return to the city I broached the mat
ter to President Neflf. Vice. President Ral
ston and others of The executive commit
tee. All agreed that it was a splendid
Idea, ami the early result was the official
invitation forwarded to Secretary Ray
! mond in January last. Considerable cor
respondence followed, and in the mean
time W. C. Ralston, during an Eastern
' visit, did yeoman work among members
I of the council. As is the proper practice
of the association, letters were addressed
to California members of the Institute,
inquiring if a meeting here would be a
success. All replies were cordially favor
able, and the result of the total efforts
made was the selection in February last
of San Francisco as the next meeting
place. The long labors Riven to the suc
ceeding preparations for the meeting by
the association are another story."

"CORDIAL WELCOME TO THE MINING
CAMP OF 1849"--MAYOR PHELAN
San Francisco owes the splendid impetus its early growth received
to the mines of California. To-day the mines are still a great source
of the prosperity of city and State, and, with the passing of primitive
methods, will contribute still more in the future by the application of
scientific knowledge by competent engineers. The visit, therefore, of the
American Institute of Mining Engineers is most timely. They are
welcome to the mining camp of 1849, which is now the metropolis of
1899. This should be for them a congenial field, and without any for
mal greeting they should feel at home. Our citizens, respecting and
honoring their profession and recalling the State's history as "the land
of gold," cordially welcomes the institute to California.
JAMES D. PHELAN, Mayor.
San Francisco, September 24.
PRESIDENT NEFF'S WORDS OF WELCOME.
THE American Institute of Mining Engineers is the most important
body and almost the only one connected with the mining industry
that has ever visited California. It comprises a membership from all
over the world, and it is composed of eminent men whose opinions are
valued everywhere. We expect that they will give our mines and min
eral resources an intelligent inspection, and we do not fear the result.
The California Miners' Association brought these distinguished
leaders of the mining industry here, and its members are highly grat
ified at their arrival. Through its executive and general reception com
mittees and its local organizations in various counties it has arranged
to offer a cordial California welcome and to show them all of the mining
resources of the State that can be seen in the limited time at the dis
posal of the vests. In behalf of the organized mining men of the State
who in these days appreciate the value of brains and intelligence in
the mining industry, I greet these guests of California.
J. H. NEFF,
President California Miners' Association.
<j> JAMES DOLGLAS, President, f
SECRETARY RAYMOND PREDICTS
MINING PROGRESS IN CALIFORNIA
RKPPTXO, Sept. 24.â "We expect
the present meeting to stir up in
terest in the Institute on the Pa
cific Coast, and that we will heai
more from the mining engineers of th(
coast in the -future," said Secretarj
Raymond to-day. "We are all. oi
course, pleased to be in California
Since I was here, a comparativelj
short time ago, there have been a
number of important developments
including the copper mine we saw to
day. A number of deep mines on thÂ«
mother lode have shown great im
provement and important develop
ments. The State's largest progress
I think, will attend the cheapening ol
operations. The great thing now is
the working of large bodies of low
grade ore. There should he a great
progress in mining in California In thÂ«
next few years, for recent develop
ments have revealed possibilities, anc
there is no reason why these discover
ies should not be repeated.
"The meetings of the institute art
largely social, the papers prepared and
presented being published promptly
and going to every member of the in
stitute, who is kept in touch with th<
best current thought and practice In
his field. The meetings foster a feel-
ing of association and fraternity in the profession and a loyalty to the institute
and they stimulate men who have acquired new knowledge to prepare reports
which they would not otherwise do. Our membership is not confined to the fields
of mining, metallurgy and economic geology with a standard of technical ac
quirements, but is open to practical mining men as well."
PUBLISHER OF IRON AGE TALKS OF TRIP.
REDDING, Sept. 24.â David Williams,
publisher of the Iron Age, in a talk with
The (Jail correspondent to-day said:
"The party with which I am visiting
California is making a summer trip of
about five weeks through some of the
great western mineral regions of the
United States, making the San Francisco
meeting of the American Institute of
Mining Engineers, In which we are
greatly interested, the excuse and oc
casion for spending this time in learning
more about the mineral industry of the
country, and in enjoying the scenic
beauties of the West. We are combining
pleasure with profit. We will not have
time for detailed study of anything, but
will acquire, I hope, an enlarged concep
tion of the country's mineral resources.
We have already been impressed with the
magnitude and importance of what little
we have seen on the trip, Including what
we saw to-day.
Having been only twenty-four hours in.
FINING -MCTALLUQ O>
J THEO. D. RAND, Treasurer.
R. W. RAYMOND, Secretary.
the State, I will say little about Califor
nia. Many of the party have said that
they are already paid for taking the trip.
Many men of the party are closely identi
fied with the silver and gold features of
this Industry, which I am not. As Jo the
possibilities of iron production on the Pa
cific Coast, of which I am asked to say
something briefly, T will simply remark
that it is a large problem which I would
rather be excused from discussing. This
coast has great and rich deposits of iron
ore, but unfortunately the coal problem
Is the biggest one. The days of charcoal
are long past, and while the water power
of this State will make a wonderfull in
dustrial development in some fields, we
have not yet got to smelting by elec
tricity. The possibilities of Chinese coal
following the development of those coal
fields with cheap labor have been dis
cussed, and 1 cannot now see why Chinese
coal cannot be- made available for smelt
ing iron in California. China is prac
tically closer to California than Mon
tana." t

CALIFORNIA ENJOYS A GREAT PROSPECTING BOOM.
A Canvass of the State Demonstrates That Unusual Activity in the Mining
Industry Prevails.
HERE is a current record that may |
suggest to judicious minds that
California is not "worked out," :
from a mining standpoint. It is
worthy of a little attention amid the
crowding facts of this State's mining in
dustry and mineral resources.
The Call herewith presents a fractional,
but still striking, record of the number of
new mining locations made in California
during; eight months of 1899. This record
is gathered from counties of all grades of
mining activity and for the period covered
the complete record would undoubtedly
exceed in averages, etc., the totals here
given.
The Call recently sent inquiries concern
ing the number of mining locations filed
and some other current features of the
industry to county officials or correspond
ents in each county. Many have not yet
replied, but the showing here made is
sufficient for the general purpose of -â¢â¢'
--ting forth one evidence of the present
mining activity in California and offering
one suggestion of the State's future min
ing possibilities.
The record, given in detail below, shows
that during eight months of the current
year (J124 mining location notices were filed |
In twenty-two of the fifty-seven counties
of the State. If this ratio were kept up :
to the end of the year there would be 9186
mining locations "recorded in only these
twenty-two counties during 1599, the aver
age for these counties being _',>. If the
iiity - two present mineral-producing ,
<'ou~nties out of the whole tifty
seven counties sustained this aver
age the number for the fifty-two
counties for eight months would be 14,466,
and f'<r the whole year 21,684. Anything
like tliis would be a pretty good record
for brand new mines, or "prospects," in
one State in one year, and it closely ap
proximates the true situation.
The r,umber of locations reported by
counties is its follows:

LASSEN.
Lassen County includes a great northern
mineral region in the Sierras and is one
of those out-of-the-way counties Buffering
from neglect through lack of ready com
munication. The report from Lassen
County includes the following: "The con
dition of the mining industry in Lassen
County is far better and more hopeful
than for ten years last past. More pros
pecting is being done and with better re
sults. More capital is being invested in
i mining properties than ever before in the
history of the county." The total number
of lode locations filed was twenty-four.
There were no placer locations. Twelve
mining properties wnre deeded and two
bonded.
BUTTB.
Butte County displays forty-one quartz
and sixty-six placer locations, a total of
107 and fifty-seven transfers of mining
properties. The placer locations have
mainly been with a view to dredging and
several gold dredgers will soon be added
to the five now operating on the Feather
River.
YUBA.
Tuba County is small and has much
fruit, but its early settled eastern end
reaches Into the mineralized Sierra Ne
vada foothills and the Yuba River pro
vides one of the coming dredging fields.
Yuba's report is:
The outlook for the mining industry in
this county has not been better for twen
ty years, although the records show that
there have been only nineteen location
notices filed, one-third the number that
were filed during the same period last
year This is accounted for by the fact
that most of the claims are located and
now the owners are working them. There
is great activity in placer mining on the
rubs River by means of dredgers. The
introduction of electricity Into several
districts has made it possible to work
profitably many mines that have lain idle
for years. Twenty sales and three bonds
have been recorded.
ALPINE.
Little Alpine County, perched up In the
Sierra Nevadas, has Its share of new min
ing activity, but only forty-five location
notices we're filed with County Clerk
Prank Smith.

LAKE.
Lake County is reported on In the fol
lowing comprehensive way by County Re
corder Frank VV. Beach:
"Mining claims filed in Lake County are
generally cinnabar claims. There have
been twenty-five claims recorded so far
in the year 1599.
"There has been no appreciable Increase
in the mining industry in Lake County
this year, although some of the old mines
have been enlarged and furnaces rebuilt,
with one new furnace at the Abbott.
"The survival or non-survival of the
State mining law will have but little ef
fect upon the industry as a whole in the
county. One group of tilings in the Mor
gan Valley section of the county, the ore
<f which is phenomenally rich, through a
misapprehension oi the exact status of
the mining law. aiv in a muddle and will
probably go into the courts to have the.
matter adjusted. One other, the Bullion,
is now in litigation, but the validity of
the State law cannot affect it. All the
Inrcer quicksilver producing mines in
Lake County, such as the Great Western,
Str.ndard. Sulphur Bank. Abbott, et al.,
have acquired title in a large measure
through agricultural patent and hence
cannot be touched by any phase of this
law." _________

refused.
"There has been much development
work during the year and all in all the
outlook for the steady and profitable ex

empire county was never brighter than
to-day. With the other mining counties
along the great lode El Dorado has suf-

INSTITUTE'S GREAT INFLUENCE ON MINING PROGRESS.
THE mining men of California and the
coast cordially welcome the visiting I
members of the American Institute of
Mining Engineers. Many prominent
representatives of the institute are not
strangers, for in ranging round the earth
in quest of treasure some have frequently
visited California and her sister States
and Territories. But it is the first time ,
that the greatest technical society in the
world has held one of its meetings west
of the Sierras. Established In 1871 and
embracing in its membership most of the j
leaders in mining and metallurgy, the
influence of the institute has been great
on mining and scientific progress, and in
every part of the world are found mem- |
bers of that gTeat organization at the
head of development work.
In accordance with the gigantic scale
on which everything is done in California,
whether welcoming veterans of war or
captains of peace, a big programme is
mapped out, which with the business ses
sions of the next three days will tax the
receptive facult.es of the visitors, jt
were better, perhaps, had they been able
to come before the foothill streams of the
early year had dwindled to the dry and
dusty channels of to-day, but if their
presence or their counsel can in any way
aid in the movement toward conservation
of water in California now in progress,
such aid will be one of the most valuable
results of their visit.
J. F. HALLORAN,
Publisher Mining and Scientific Press-

AUCTION SALES.
By Pullivnn ft Doyle â Wednesday at 11 a. m., Buergies, etc.,
A DEEP INSIDE OF PROSPERITY
rEW persons, even those in active trade, realize |
how many things' have advanced in price during
the past few months, and particularly during the
past few weeks. Exclusive of the great staples, the
list embraces ail sorts of articles, even to writing
paper, lead pencils and pins, so that it now costs more
money to oven order goods than formerly, not to
mention paying for. them. Almost everything that the
housewife uses has gone up, as the head of the family
is ling out to his disgust, and it costs considerably
more to run the family than it did a year ago. And.
the end is apparently not yet. No such rise in com
modities has been seen since the civil war, nearly forty:
ago.
The money, market is beginning to show signs of
strain on account of this general advance in the neces
saries of life. The circulation is being drawn upon to
pay for goods and the New York banks find their
legal reserve? shrinking under the drain, especially as
the interior banks are drawing on the New York
banks for cash funds to move the crops. The Govern
ment has helped the situation by paying the October
bond interest in advance, as on former similar occa
sions, and the banks themselves will find other means
of relief, so there is no cause for alarm. The case is
cited merely to show that even prosperity sometimes
causes financial stringency, just as it threw a large
number of men out of work a fortnight or so ago be
cause the shipbuilding works could not get the raw
material to work on, owing to the immense demand
from all over the country. Both cases are peculiar,
if not unique.
The present advance in prices has another curious
feature. It will be news to -many that the manufac
turer, in spite of the general advance in his wares, is '
deriving very little benefit from it. It appears that the
rise in raw products has been so sharp that the maim l
focturer his been unable to advance his selling, prices
for manufactured goods in a corresponding ratio, for
while the latter are much higher than for some time
they have not kept pace with the increased price of '
ra.v material, and, what is of equal importance, the
rise in wages. This is prominently exhibited in the
woolen trade and many other lines. Thus, while
labor has advanced from 10 to 15 per cent boots and
shoes have risen only 4 per cent, while leather has
gone up 8 per cent. Wool has advanced 10.9
per cent, but woolen goods only 5.2 per cent,
which, in view of the IO@IS per cent increase in la
bor, gives the manufacturer less benefit than appears
at the fir: I glance. Everything put up in tin has ad
vanced, but the enormous rise in tin. combined with
the increase in wages, cuts down the packer's profits
materially. And so on. down the whole list of com
modities. From this it would appear that the pro
ducer and wnge-e:<rner are getting much more out of
the current prosperity than the manufacturer, who is
popularly supposed to get the lion's <=harÂ» of every
thing.
For the rest the situation exhibits no new features.
The weekly commercial reports sing the same unvary
ing song of marked gains in bank clearings, decreased
failures, a great wholesale and jobbing trade in almost
every line of goods, feverish activity in all branches
of the iron and steel trades and their subdivisions,,
with prices for merchandise of all descriptions mount
ing skyward. Of the eighty-three American cities
and towns reporting bank clearings only eight
showed a decrease last week, while the average gain i
all over the country was 4,1 per cent, and every large !,
city except Omaha recorded a gain over 1898. The
failures were 147, against I&2 - m the same week last ;
year.
The conditions on this coast are merely a reflex
of the wonderful prosperity general throughout the
other parts of the country. They are familiar to all
who keep in touch with trade conditions and have
been recounted time and again. Suffice it to say
that business is better in California to-day than for
a exeat many years, and that there are no complaints j
from any quarter.
Several prominent Democrats of Massachusetts'
have declined renominntions for State positions. This j
seems to be another verification of the belief that I
there are some people that have conscientious scruples j
against taking anything that is worth nothing. I
CONGRESS AND THE COLD STANDARD.
WITH the opening of the fall campaigns, pub
lic interest in all political questions revives
and renewed attention is paid to issue? which
have to some extent been overlooked by reason of
the general concern in the questions raised by the
war in the Philippines. Among these issues that of
confirming the gold standard and reforming the
currency laws of" the nation naturally comes first,
and it is gratifying to note that despite opposition
from a certain class of Republicans, the sentiment of
the party and its leaders and representatives is
strongly in favor of the enactment of a comprehen
sive act upon the subject at the coming session of
Congress.
Representative McLeary of Minnesota, who is one
of the best informed men in Congress on currency
questions and who is in rlc=e touch with influential
Republicans in the House, has recently declared a
sanguine expectation that the issue will be one of
the first to be taken up during the session, and that
the plan outlined by the Atlantic City conference
will be substantially adopted. That plan it will be
remembered includes a declaration for the gold
standard so formulated That only by act of Congress
can the monetary standard be changed, the protec
tion of the gold reserve from what has been termed
the "endless chain." the authorization of bank note
issues to the par value of the United States bonds
deposited as security, and the establishment of banks
in less populated communities on a reduced capital.
The Republican State conventions which have
been held this year have strongly and clearly de
ciared for the gold standard and a scientific revision
of our currency laws. Moreover these platform
promises have been emphatically repeated by the
candidates nominated upon them. From one end of
the country to the other the voice of sterling Re
publicanism has been unmistakable on that point,
and the few time-serving politicians who counsel
delay or compromise have had no influence what
ever either in the party conventions or in the cam
paigns.
In opening the Republican canvass in Ohio on Sat
urday Judge Nash reminded the voters of the State
that the money question is still one of the issues of
the politics of the country' and could not be evaded
either by the people or by Congress. "The Demo
cratic party." lie said, "continues to demand the free
and unlimited coinage of silver and gold as equal
in primary money at the ratio of 16 to I," and if
Ohio give its vote to that party the effect would be
to revive the whole agitation and once more disturb
the finances and the industries of the country. "Our
prosperity, 'â  he added, "is due to the determination
of the American people to maintain inviolate and
forever a sound financial policy in this land.
By the elections in 1806 confidence was restored in
the financial integrity of the people of the United
States. Hence, those who controlled capital were
willing to let it seek investment in railroads, manu
factures and commerce. The unemployed are no
longer idle. Those who worked only one-half time
and for half pay are now reaping a full harvest."
These stalwart declarations for the right are the
more gratifying because, as the Philadelphia Public
Ledger has pointed out: "Certain Republican jour
nalists and politicians profess that the currency laws
are good enough as they are: that what is essential
is the retention of the Republican party in power and
the maintenance of revenue laws that shall avert a
deficiency in the Treasury. This view of the situa
tion i? "childlike and bland" rather than convincing.
It ignores the possibility of contingencies 4 that can
not definitely be anticipated in time to make prep
aration for unusual demands on the Treasury."
The business interests of the country require a
prompt settlement of the whole money question.
The gold standard must be confirmed and the con
fusion in the currency laws brought to order by the
adoption of a scientific and sound monetary system.
That is one of the thing 3 expected of Congress at
the coming session, and it is encouraging to note
that all signs point to the conclusion the expectation
will be realized.
Chicago i? having such a big silver punchbowl pre
pared for the battleship Illinois that the people there,
do not speak of having it made but of having it
erected. It is going to be soriiething monumental,
and it would seem that in an emergency it might be
used as a lifeboat.
Mrs. Mary Brooks, the first woman sentenced to
the Michigan State prison at Jackson, is about lo
have her revenge on the man whose testimony sent
her up. She has consented to marry him.
EXPANSION AND COTTON
SENATOR MORGAN has addressed a com
mercial argument to the Southern cotton
planters to show their interest in imperialism,
lie says that "the time is coming when an American
city in the Pacific islands will handle two-thirds of
the cotton in the world."
The Tacoma Ledger is charmed with this prospect
and observes that "thousands of bales of American
cotton have been shipped from Tacoma in the last
two years, and cotton manufacturing machinery is
going the same way. The Asiatics are the great
est consumers of cotton in the world, and instead
of relying on Manchester are beginning to manu
facture thrir own goods."
It seems, then, that we are to rely on the produc
tion of raw material to be manufactured by the cheap
skilled labor of Asia. Perhaps the Ledger will cite
the example of any nation that has grown rich on the
production of raw material. The foundation of the
Republican theory of protection is the stimulation of
manufacture, not the production of raw material to
be transmuted by the skill of others. A=ia and
Africa always had cotton. When Europe was dress
ing in the skins of animals, and later when the people
of the Western nations wore coarse woollen fabrics,
Asia and Africa had the finest cotton and linen. The
cerements of Egyptian mummies, laid away three
thousand years ago, are of cotton and linen, so finely
woven as to prove the hifiheÂ«t skill in manufacture
and the production of the raw fiber.
Senator Morgan's American city in the Pacific
islands will not long handle American cotton after
the manufacturing machinery is put in operation.
There are vast reaches of land in Asia and Africa,
virgin and unexhausted, that will produce cotton. It
is a law of economics that where nature permits the
production, the raw material and its manufacture
come close together. The planting of cotton mills in
Asia to be run with cheap labor means the raising of
Asiatic raw cotton by still cheaper labor, and our
expansion, achieved at great cost, will mean the
transfer of an industry from the mjdst of us to the
mid<=t of the cheap labor of the Orient.
The Ledger declares that "if any people in this
country should be interested in the retention of the
Philippines it is the cotton growers of the South."
That is true, but their interest is against such reten
tion if it be to introduce the very changes in Asia
foretold by Morgan and the Ledger.
Our cotton trade with Asia and pceanica for the

SOUTH AMERICAN ALARMISTS.
SINCE the outburst of foolish boasting on the
part of the imperialists of the country there
have come to us many reports of the alarm
felt in South America lest in our lust for conquest
and dominion we set about subverting first Mexico
| and afterward all the country southward until we in
â  elude among our colonial possessions the whole of
j Spanish America.
An alarm of the kind is so unreasonable the re-
I ports have been regarded in this country with mi
i difference and dismissed from consideration as idle
! rumors. They have now been repeated so often,
however, and come to us from so many different
sources that it is just as well for us to give heed to
them. It is true all such fears on the part of Mexico
or any Central or South American country are with
out foundation, but so long as any considerable num
ber of people feel them the effect upon the interna-
I tional relations between ourselves and our southern
| neighbors is bound to be great.
We desire the commerce of South America, and
trade, like kissing, "goes by favor."' The Euro
peans, who are our competitors in the markets of
Spanish America, will of course make use of every
possible appeal to prejudice to stimulate antagonism
to the United States for the purpose of checking the
consumption of our goods as far a? possible. In
that respect the rumors of a probable attempt at
subverting the independence of the South American
states may do a great deal of harm in more ways
than one.
This feeling of uneasiness among a people with
whom we desire friendly relations is one of the evil
results of the jingo boasting over the proposed policy
of commercial expansion by means of imperial con
j quests. Senseless in itself, that kind of talk has given
rise to senseless fears, and it may require years of
diplomacy to overcome the bad effects, which have
already become of no small magnitude. Folly at
home begets folly abroad, and out of the two there
come manifold injuries to the true interests of both
parties.
In reality every American knows the states to the
south of us have nothing to fear from our aggression
and everything to expect from our friendship. The
trade between the two continents of this hemisphere
ought to be large, and it is our duty to take the
initiative in making it so, and yet it is our own swag
gering imperialists who are giving occasion for the
fears which are engendering the South American
antagonisms of which we are now hearing so much.
THE DEADLY THIRD RAIL.
FROM reports that come to us from the East con
cerning the number of fatal accidents caused by
the third rail which is now coming into use on
electric railroads in that section it is evident that in
the new system of traction we have another of those
deadly forces which constitute a menace to civilized
communities.
Recently a track-walker on the Brooklyn Elevated
Railroad stumbled and fell, and as his body came into
contact with the third rail he was instantly killed.
Commenting upon the accident the New York
Tribune says: "The incident was a startling reminder
that this third rail, which is so useful, which looks sj
innocent and which is so accessible to the touch of
accident or thoughtlessness, is charged with lethal
energy so great that the slightest touch thereof is
death. In case of a blockade on the elevated road,
which is not an infrequent occurrence, passengers are
wont to leave a stalled train and walk along the tracks
to the nearest station. That formerly could be done
with safety. Now they must walk within a few inches
of a thunderbolt. The rail runs close by station plat
j forms, where one may easily touch it with the tip of
a walking stick, and where one accidentally slipping
down upon the track or getting down to recover some
object that had fallen thither would almost certainly
come in contact with it. Such are the perils on an
elevated road. On a surface road these would of
course be greatly increased and multiplied."
On this coast we have had no experience as yet
with the third rail system. It is only a matter of time,
however, when it will be introduced here, for it is of
great advantage in electric roads. It is as well, there
fore, for us to give heed now to the dangers that ac
company its use in order to guard against them when
any application is made for the right to use the sys
tem either in the cities or along country highways.
Efforts are now being made in the East to miti
gate the danger of the system, and doubtless good
results will be obtained. It is believed it will be prac
ticable eventually to use a rail which will carry no
electric charge save at the moment and the point of
contact with the car motor. Should that be accom
plished the third rail will be the best of electric sys
tems now known, but until that time comes it is best
to leave the dangerous third rail alone or else hedge
its use with the most stringent safeguards.

ing to this State to give our fruit-growers informa
tion as to the best means of cultivating the Smyrna
fig. and it will not be inappropriate to wish the State
msÂ«r give the nation many a fig for it in return.
Another submarine boat has been "successfully
tested," and it is possible she may be of some use in
war, but the record of other craft of her kind that
were tested with equal success is not encouraging.

i Local Weather Office Complimented

The officials In charge of the local
! Weather Bureau are in receipt of a highly
j complimentary notice from Willis L.
! Moore, chief of the head office in Wash-
I ington, D. C, regarding the efficiency of
I the service in this city. While appreci
ating the honor done them they are ear
nestly hoping that the words of . praise
are not the forerunner of anything seri
ous in the way of upheavals in office. The
letter is particularly flattering to Section
Official Alexander McAdie, who assumed
charge of the local office a few months
i ago. It is as follows:
j United States Department of Agriculture
Weather Bureau.
Mr. Alexander G. McAdie, Forecast Official
L and Section Director. San Franciscn, Cal.â
I Sir: The recent reports of the California sec
| tion, especially those for May, Jun? and July.
! contain a vast amount of climatological in
! formation presented In a most interesting
j manner, and I wish to say that the publica
â¢ tions are a great credit to you and the force
at your station. The diagrams In the May and
July reports illustrating the rainfall at San
i Francisco and Sacramento respectively are
| especially Interesting. The plan of the illus
; tratlon will be readily understood by those
| familiar with the methods of constructing such

Chief United States Weather Bureau.

! ferred to, which will include the whole
' State, is now being rapidly pushed for
' ward, and the reports for Los Angeles
: and San Diego covering the rainfall dur
' ing the last fifty years will soon be ready.
Yesterday was a "scorcher" on the top
jof Mount Tamalpais, the thermometer
I registering 92 degrees, while it was as low
as 56 in this city, a difference of 46 de
grees. Professor McAdie is hard at work

NEWS OF FOREIGN NAVIES.
A new type of torpedo boat for the
German navy has just been launched from
Shichan's yard at Elbing. It is 223 feet
in length. 9 feet draught and has a trial
speed of 36 knots. Until further improve
: ments suggest themselves this type will
j be the standard torpedo boat in the Ger-
I man- navy.
The Italian battleships Ttalia and T.e
panto, of 14.400 tons, are to be stripped of
their armor and converted into cruisers.
j The gain by removal of armor will be
utilized in coal stowage, and the new
bunkers, sixteen feet wide, will afford
! great protection to the machinery. The
Italia was built in 1880 and the T.epanto
three years later, and they were very
; speedy ships. Converted Into cruisers,
| their four 100-ton guns will be removed
and four ten-inch and a number of quick
firing six-inch guns will be substituted.
The Pallada. a protected cruiser of 6731
; tons, was launched August 27 from the
' new admiralty yard. Galierny Island. St.
j Petersburg. She is 416 feet in length. 54
. feet 4 Inches beam and 21 feet draft. The
machinery, triple expansion engines and
twenty-four Belleville boilers, is to de
' velop 11.610 horsepower, to give a speed of
! twenty knots, and the armament is com-
I posed of eight six-inch, twenty-four three
-1 inch quick firing guns and eight six
! pounders, and the vessel has also two
broadside and one bow torpedo tube. The
Diana and Aurora, sister ships to the Pal-
I lada, are still on the stocks at Galierny
! Island, although they were begun early
j in 1897.
The trouble with the British battleship
! Ocean has been discovered to be in the
! cracked high-pressure cylinder of the
l port engine, and extensive repairs and
considerable loss of time will be entailed
!at the dockyard. As the vessel has not
yet been accepted by the admiralty the
loss falls on the contractors. Few ships
in the British navy pass through their
trials without accidents or defects being
found in their machinery. In contrast to
this, it Is exceedingly rare that United
States vessels meet with accidents during
their trials or that defects of a serious
nature are developed, such being chiefly
confined to the torpedo boats, and these
are proportionately less than what hap
pens to British boats.
Peter the Great's first attempt to utilize
the navy he had built and organized with
so much trouble was a signal failure. The
first squadron was sent to sea In 1713 to
fight Russia's old enemy, Sweden, in -the
Baltic, and three Swedish vessels of in
ferior force were overtaken. During the
engagement the Russian vice admiral's
ship and that of the senior captain com
modore ran aground, and the junior cap
tain commodore failed to render assist
ance, and the Swedes escaped. For this
the two senior Russian officers were de
graded a-nd the junior captain commodore
I was sentenced to be shot. At the last
I moment, when the culprit stood blindfold
| ed awaiting thy volley from the executton
| ers, the! death .sentence was remitted, but
the fright he had experienced had upset
his reason, and he lingered but a few
years in Siberia, a mental wreck. The
! early Russian navy had many Scotchmen
I and Englishmen in it as officers, one of
I the latter, named Nelson, serving as cap
i tain, and was a member of the first court
â¢ martial which gave the Russian officers
j a taste of naval discipline.
Germany has been rather unsuccessful
iin its earlier armored shipbuilding. Four
', second-class battleships, or rather coast
i defense vessels, built in 1878 and 1880,
i named Baden, Bayern, Sachsen and Wur
; temberg. of 7441 tons, were practically fail
ures, and had to be almost reconstructed
three years ago. The German navy also
possesses six coast defense ships of 3500
I tons of the monitor type, but having high
I unprotected upper works, which make the
; vessels unseaworthy and absolutely
I worthless as lighting vessels. They were
| built in 1890-1-2-3, and although rated at
: sixteen knots they are in reality slow
| ships. Their coal capacity is only 225
tons, and In order to remedy this defect
jit has been decided to reconstruct the
; Hagen, launched in 1893. It is intended to
| cut the vessel in two and put in twenty
five feet additional hull between the two
; ends, thereby increasing the coal-carrying
| capacity. The upper works will also be
j greatly reduced. If the plan proves a
I success with ;he Hagen the other vessels
! of that class, namely, Beowulff, Heimdal
Hildebrand, Frlthjoy and Siegfried, will
be similarly reconstructed.
SHOT THROUGH
A DOOR TWICE
AT HER LODGER
Warm Reception for
Schwebs.
TRIED TO FORCE HIS WAY IN
â â¢ â â
MRS.LILLIE GRAY DEFENDS HER
HOME WITH A REVOLVER,
â¦
She Is Arrested on a Charge of As
sault With a Deadly Weapon
and He Is Also in ,
Custody.
Â«
Mrs. Lillie Gray, a young widow living
at 462% Tehama street, created a mild
sensation in the neighborhood yesterday
afternoon by firing two shots through
a door at Charles C. Schwebs, who had
been trying to force an entrance into her
room. ".::!'
Mrs. Gray was left a widow about a
year ago, and as she was acquainted
I with the Schwebs family, Schwebs went
to live in her house to help her along. He
I has been employed as a tin-roofer for
nearly twenty years by Conlin & Rob
erts, on Mission street. Mrs. Gray and
Schwebs had reason to disagree lately
and she . got him to leave the house.
Early yesterday morning he went to her
house drunk and because she refused to
admit him he commenced to hammer at
the front door with the object of break
ing his way in. He was arrested, but
only a charge of "drunk" was booked
! against him and he was discharged by
i Judge Graham. . >
As soon as Schwebs regained his liberty
I he commenced to drink again and went
| in the afternoon to Mrs. Gray's house.
i She again refused to admit him and ho
I began to break in the door. Exasperated
! at hi% conduct she fired two shot from a
revolver through the door, both missing
him.
Schwebs, thoroughly frightened, ran
Into ..a saloon adjoining the house and
made his way to the rear, climbing to
, the roof. He ran over the roof, dropped
onto a shed in the rear of the house on
j the other side of the saloon and took
I refuge in the basement, where he was
I found by Policemen T. P. Riordan and
John A. Ryan, who had been notified of
the shooting. The officers placed him
j under arrest, and on hearing his story
I went to Mrs. Gray's house and arrested
| her. The revolver was taken as evi
l dence against her.
Captain Spillane ordered a charge of as
i sault with a deadly weapon to be booked,
j against Mrs. Gray and a charge of dis
-1 turbing the peace against Schwebs.
Schwebs declared with tears in his eyes
i that he did not want to prosecute the
} woman, as he loved her still, although
she preferred a soldier. He offered her
his hand in a friendly way, but she
scorned him and said she â¢wanted to have
nothing further to do with him.
"I only fired the shots at him," said
Mrs. Gray, "to scare him, as he had
I twice attempted to break in my door. I
i had no intention of injuring him. I had
not seen him for some weeks till ha
called in a drunken condition early yes
terday morning."
Mrs. Gray's boy, 4 years of age, was
I taken to the City Prison with his mother
and was placed in charge of the matron.
He was run down by a wagon recently
and Is still suffering from the injuries
he then sustained.

A Queer War Incident.
Speaking of these short rushes forward,
a queer thing happened at Guguinto,
where perhaps half of the soldiers in one
regiment found hens. Before there waa
time to kill the fowl orders came to go
forward. A little farther out the regiment
lay down under fire for some minutes.
I Then the order came to charge. Up nnd
i forward with a yell rushed the soldiers,
! but over the cheering rose another sound.
i Three or four hundred hens, objecting to
I being carried head down with feet tied to
j the strap of a haversack, set up a frantic
squawking such as was probably never
heard along a line of charging military
before. â Manila Correspondent in Leslie's
Weekly.
