INSURGENTS ARE TO BLAME
Â«
AN INTERESTING LECTURE AT
ST. BRENDAN'S CHURCH.
The First California's Chaplain
Praises Officers and Men in the
Philippines for Their Re
â¢ spect for Religion.
â¢
The Rev. Father McKinnon, chaplain of
the First California Regiment, took occa
sion, in his lecture on "The Churches of
Manila" at St. Brendan's Church last â 
night, to vindicate the American soldiers
of the charge of looting the churches of
the islands, which has indirectly been laid
at their door. - Father McKinnon's lan
guage was positive and his praise of i
American officers and men unstinted. He i
Raid In part:
Aguinaldo and his followers, with thÂ»
oath-bound organization to which they be
long, have no use for religion, and they
have Imprisoned ninny of the pastors of
the churches. It may not be out of place i
to say a word regarding the alleged looting !
â ad sacrilegious ration of the churches i
by our soldiers that we read so much about
in the dally, and especially the religious,
press. That the American soldiers are to
blame for the desecration I most emphati
cally deny. Every precaution was taken
during the campaign. A general order, read
ejght days in succession to every command,
was Issued by General Otis against it. I i
am proud to say every commander did his
best to enforce this.
I was with all the commands. I received .
strict orders to take whatever guard was
needed to protect the churches and was told
that I would be responsible for whatever
ration took place. What more could
a commander do? In most of the towns the !
churches were dismantled before our en- j
trance. In regard to the church at Caloo- i
can. i visited it some months before the ]
battle at that place and found It a veritable
fortress and used as such by the Insurgents.
This church the American shells demolished
to dislodge the Insurgents. The American
army has been unjustly criticized In this I
matter.
Father McKinnon Illustrated his lecture
with stereopticon views from photo
graphs which he took while on the Isl
unds. Th. lecture was full of interest
arid was heard by a crowded house. j
A BIG DAY PARADE
Minnesota Volunteers at the Circus
To-Morrow Night.
It was all cirrus last night. The down
town population was decreased by just
as many as could be crowded into the
seats that the Walter Main show people
have put up under their big tent at Six
teenth and Folsom streets. Th. entertain
ment bore out the promise that was made
on Saturday night in the bis street parade
and initial performance, and, as on the
first night, the Rough Riders and the ring
full of performing norses were the prin
cipal attractions, that will draw another
tentful of spectators to-night. Both the
matinee and the night performance were
the best that the Main troupe is able to
furnish, and both were equally well at
tended.
To-morrow night is to be Minnesota
nUht. Something like 1000 men of the
Minnesota regiment have been provided
with tickets and' will attend In a body. For
Wednesday another big street parade has
been arranged. It will start from the big
tf-nts at 9:30 o'clock in the morning, and
JmJjjing- from the line 'of march that has
been outlined it won't get back there un
til time for the afternoon show. . In that
parade the whole menagerie will partici
pate, every case will be open and every
attraction on exhibition. It will start from
the lot at Sixteenth and Folsom streets,
and will go down to East street, to the
ferry, then up Market to Kearny and out
to Pacific; down Dupont to Market again,
and out to McAllister, and then out L.ar
kin to Post. From Post it will go out
Polk to Jackson, then down Van Ness to
Hayes, then to Ninth and up Mission to
Sixteenth, when it will return to the lot.
It will be the biggest circus parade San
Francisco has ever seen, and with the at
tractions the Mains hold out it will be
well worth seeing.

ANOTHER WEEK OF
UNLIGHTED STREETS
FOR another weok San Francisco's streets at night will preserve the
pitchy blackness that has been their chief characteristic since the gas
company shut off the light? last Friday.
The profound jtloom is more than an irritating inconvenience â it is
a peril. us rat nace to the safety of the city's inhabitants. For six more
nights it will continue, and for six mon- nights belated pedestrians
will blindly start- at poles and fence corners in anxious expectation of meet
ing a lurking: footpad.
The most efficient police force Is powerless to cope with such conditions.
Thorough illumination is accounted by criminal authorities one of the most
potent aids to the arm of the law. However, as the city will not pay the bills
the company will not furnish the gas. Therefore not until the Ist of October
will the gas jets again twinkle cheerfully on the street corners of San Fran
cisco.
other cases are of murder, accident,
drowning and railroad disasters.
On the evening of September 22 William
Carr. a can maker, residing at S4.i Mission
street, and a soldier named William
Flannigan were taken to the city Receiv
ing Hospital in police patrol wagons for
medical treatment. Carr was picked up
on the corner of Sixth and Bryant and
Flannigan was found on the corner of
Fifth and Market streets. Both were un
conscious and died soon after reaching the
hospital. It appears, that no examination
\v:is made by the city doctors as to the
cause of their collapse, the victims having
been treated for alcoholism. When the
Morgue deputies arrived to take posses
sion of the bodies they were given to un
derstand that the agent of death in both
ces was alcohol, and it was so
stated in the death report.
On the following daj an autopsy was
performed on The remains of Carr. The
startling disclosure was then made that
the man had come to his death by a frac
tured skull and not from drink. This fact
was unknown to the hospital authorities.
When the body of Flannigan, who was
a soldier, was taken to the Presidio, it
was found that there were several clots
Of M-iod pressing on his brain and that
one of these had produced death. As in
the case of Carr the hospital physicians
did not know of their presence and calmly
allowed the man to die while they were
treating him for an overindulgence in
alcoholic stimulants. The cases will be
brought to the attention of a Coroner's
lury to-day and Detective Gibson, who
has been making an Investigation for the
police, will give his testimony.
An inquest will be held on the body of
Ixmise Schlegel, the young and unfor
tunat< domestic who was accidentally as
phyxiated by gas. The death of William
Kelly, who " was found in the hay last
week will also be Investigated. The mur
der of William Kennedy. wh.> was shot
by "Tack" Main in a Third-street sali>on,
will be called to the attention of a jury,
as will be the death of John E. Burke,
who was fatally injured In an explosion
on the transport Sherman last Friday
night. The last case is that of G. W. Wil
liams, who died in the Southern Pacific
Railroad Hospital from injuries received
in a railroad wreck in Nevada. He was a
LOtive fireman and was pinned be
neath a mass of wreckage in a collision.

COLORED MAN
TRIES TO KILL
A POLICEMAN
â¦
Bloody Encounter in
a Saloon.
â¢ , -
GEIMAN'S NARROW ESCAPE
â â¢ â¦
HIS ASSAILANT MAKES AN AT
TEMPT AT SUICIDE.
â¦
After a Desperate Struggle the In
furiated Man Is Subdued With
the Butt End of a
Revolver.
Â«
After slashing Police Officer Geiman
over the right eye last night with a |
razor, "Andy"" Campbell, a colored piano
mover, drew the blade across his own
throat, inflicting a serious wound. De
spite the injury Campbell fought viciously
J against arrest, and the officer found it
, necessary to beat him into submission
with the butt end of his revolver.
Geiman was patrolling his beat on
Grant avenue when his attention was
called to. Campbell, who- was standing in
front of a saloon at 205 Post street, nour
! ishing a razor and threatening to. carve
the first one who interfered with him.
The officer accosted Campbell and en
deavored to coax him to give him the |
razor. Campbell, after threatening to
j send Geimaii to the Morgue if he attempt- ;
I ed to disarm him, backed into the saloon.
. Geiman followed him and again tried to !
! Induce him to give up the razor.
With an oath the desperate colored man
; made a slash at the policeman, cutting
, him over the eye. Quickly drawing his ,
| club, Geiman struck his assailant over
the head several times, forcing him
against the wall.
Just then Campbell drew the keen blade i
of the razor across his own throat, in
flicting a deep wound.
Thinking that he was unable to further '
resist arrest, Geiman took out his hand
cuffs, intending to place them on the ne
gro's wrists.
"You ain't got me yet," screamed the
infuriated man, as he sprang at the officer i
and slashed at him with the blood-stained
1 razor. Fortunately Goiman. who is one j
of the most active men In the department,
got out of the way of the razor, and draw
ing his revolver proceeded to beat Camp- <
bell over the head with it.
With the assistance of several bystand
ers the officer finally subdued the mur
derous piano mover. He was then hand
cuffed and removed to the Receiving Hos- .
pital, where the wound In his throat was I
stitched by Drs. Cluness and Hopper.
Getman was also treated at the hospital.
His uniform was cut in several places, |
showing that the razor wielder meant
business.
On the advice of lieutenant Birdsall
the injured policeman caused a charge of j
an assault to commit murder to be placed I
against Campbell. v

AN OLD MAN KILLED.
William Phillips Struck by a South
â¢ crn Pacific Train.
William Phillips, a native of Ireland,
aged 55 years, was found dead at 6 o'clock
yesterday morning at the crossing of
Ninth and Division streets. When dis
covered by Thomas Hughes, a special
officer belonging 1 to the Southern Pacific
Company, the body was lying alongside
of the : railway track with both arms
mangled near the shoulders, blood trick
ling from the ears and mouth. From the
position of the body it is evident that the
unfortunate had been struck by a passing
train during the previous night.
Phillips had been a resident of the sec
tion of the city where he was killed for a
number of years. He had been engaged
In looking after an old building- belonging
to Joe Shearer and lived in a portion of
the old shed for years. â¢ â -.":â  â¢-..â¢;
The body was removed to the Morgue
by Deputies Lacombe and Smith,

NEW OPERA BY
H. J. STEWART
AT THE GRAND
It is gratifying- to hear that the man
agementfl of the various theaters are be
ginning to give local composer* an oppor
tunity to bo heard. The announcement
that Max Hirschfeld's errand opera would
bo produced at the Tivoli "before the close
of the present season is followed by the
statement that a comic opera by Dr. H.
J. Stewart will be presented at the Grand
Opera House some time next month, and
rehearsals will in all probability begin
this week. The opera is in the Spanish
style of music and the libretto is some
what bouffe. It consists of three acts and
Is said to be very catchy. Dr. Stewart
had an opera performed in this city some
years ago which was quite successful,
it ml no doubt this present work will be
expected with much interest.
Owing to a great demand for seats the
Tivoli management has found Itself
obliged to continue the grand opera season
Indefinitely. This evening "Martha" will
be given, with Thomas Green, the new
tenor, who appeared with the Castle
Square Opera Company last season and
scored quite a success. This lighter opera
will alternate with "Othello." which seems
to have captured the San Francisco pub
lic. An item of intense interest to our lo
cal musicians is the fact that among the
operas in preparation is Verdi's latest
work. "Falstaff," which made a tre
mendous hit in European musical circles.
It is said by many authorities that "Fal
staff" is the grand composer's best work
The concert given by Miss Grace Car*
roll in Oakland last Friday evening was
very successful. The young lady has a
very large voice, which will make a mark
for her after it has been fully cultivated.
It already shows many signs of rare tim

NEW STEAMER LINE.
Several transports will sail this week.
The Gfant will depart to-day for Manila.
The Sikh and the Rio "Will sail to-day for
Portland to take on board the Thirty-flfth
United States Infantry for Manila. The
Sheridan will sail next Saturday.
The coming to port yesterday of the
steamer Valencia started a lot of launch
es used for excursion purposes in her di
rection as she lay off Goat Island. The
boatmen reaped a harvest taking passen
gers to the Valencia and the lowa.
SAN DIKGO, Sept. 24.â The San Diego
Union will say to-morrow morning that
a new steamship line, in which San Diego
is Interested, will soon he operating.
Somo of. the steumers will i;un to Central
American ports and others will majce
monthly trips to the Hawaiian islands.
San Diego or San Francisco will be the
terminal point. The steamers will be run
in connection with the Santa Fe road. The
J. S. Kimball Company of San Francisco
will manage the line. Whether the ter
minal point will be San Diego or San
Francisco has not been decided. Three
steamers are on the stocks for the new
venture. One is ready to launch. The ves
sels will be the finest of their class ever
built on the Pacific Coast. The one near
ly completed is almost 235 feet long. The
speed capacity of all the fleet will be fif
teen knots. Two are building at Grays
Harbor and one is nearly completed at
Puget Sound. General Manager Nevins
of the Santa Fe is in the East, and he
may purchase two fast steamers there to
run between San Diego and Honolulu
alone, connecting with the Kimball line
for San Francisco.
The removal of the lightship from the
bar badly puzzled Captain Lewis of the
James A." Gftrfield, and may prove to be
a serious matter for others. The Gar
field last Wednesday was on its way from
the north with a cargo of lumber for this
port. In the midst of the fog the vessel
got into the breakers off the North Far
allon. Sheering off northeast by east,
Noonday Rock was cleared. After he had
passed the Farallones Captain Lewis
searched for the lightship, but found only
a buoy in its place.
The transport Leelanaw reported on its
arrival yesterday speaking the ship Ar
thur Sewall on Saturday. This is the
maiden voyage of the Sewall from Balti
more.
SUNDAY AT THE PARK.
The crowds at the beach and the park
were large yesterday morning, but before
the hour arrived for the bands to play
and the fun in general to bog-In the fog
bank drfven landward by a chilling breeze
caused most of the people to seek, their
home?. A T ery few remained at the park
after the intermission in the musiral pro
gramme, and the beach was likewise de
serted about that hour.
Scorchers at the park pave the police
some trouble yesterday, and the police
reciprocated. J. W. Edmonds, J. A. Tay
lor, H. Berrar. Otto Koch, Frank Holland
p.nd S. J. Thomas were arrested for fast
riding and taken to the station. Each
was' compelled to deposit $20 ball before
his freedom was granted. All managed
' to raise the necessary funds and secured
their release.
As shelter is afforded, the fog did not
drive the crowd from Â«he Chutes, several
thousand people remaining- there during
the entire day. Adgie and the high div
ing act were the features of the day.

BOHE PROVES
A WONDER AT
UNION PARK
Romps Through the Open
Stake, Defeating Imported
Plough Boy.
J. H. Perigo's California-bred greyhound
Bohe, representing the best blood in this
Ftate, fairly romped through the open
Stake "at Union Coursing Park yesterday.
She defeated Sardonic, Metallic by a score
of f'iur points to nothing, Lucky Dog and
Plough Boy. She was favored with two
byes, the first an accidental one through
the withdrawal of Lord Byron, and the
Becond a natural bye. In the course with
Lucky Dog the latter led up, but Bohe
secured possession of the hare and out
worked her opponent.
The final course was a grand one, of
which the spectators saw but little, owing
to a heavy fog which obscured the field.
Bohe led from the slips by over four
lengths. The hare then turned back
toward the slipper's stand, where the dogs
worked it for some time. It then made
fur the escape, Plough Boy in the mean
time having possession of the game. The
spectators cheered both logs as they dis
appeared In the fog, and then stood peer
ing anxiously Into the mist to catch the
lirst glimpse of the flag signaling which
dog had yon. It was only a few moments.
but it seemed an interminable time, until
riag Steward Grlswold came galloping
across the field like one of Roosevelt's
Hough Riders, displaying the red nag de
noting victory for 80l
1' J Reilly's Plough Boy proved himself
a great greyhound. He was unfortunate
in having two undecided courses with Kid
McCoy before lie finally disposed of him.

Spotting Svents from All Over the World.
SACRAMENTO
AND OAKLAND
BREAK EVEN
Harvey Found for Bunches of
Kits â Morning Game
Taken by Senators.
Oakland 8, Sacramento 3.
The baseball game yesterday afternoon
at Recreation grounds resulted in a
victory for Oakland because the Dudes
untwisted Harvey's shoots and curves,
not because of . their general playing.
Seven 'errors of the refulgent order wer.e
committed by the Brummels, but their
stick work was of such brilliancy that
even Cal Ewing has forgotten all about
them. Three two-baggers and a bunch
of singles were all the Dudes did to Har
vey. The crowd, which was about the
largest of the season, was happy In the
extreme.
Umpire William Long was again upon
the scene. He has not improved in the
discernment of a ball from a strike or a
baseball player from a section of Recrea
tion Park air. The spectators helped to
persuade him of this before the game was
finished.
Sacramento took a winning stride at
the beginning of the game. Mclaughlin
found a safe resting place at the first
bag when Schmeer fumbled his hit.
Harvey was given a base on balls and
O'Connor collided with the ball, tilling
all three bags. Eagan hit to Schmoer,
who threw the ball home, thus forcing
and retiring McLaughlin. Hammond.
In an attempt for a double, threw wildly
to first and Harvey scored. In the next
Inning Oakland tied the score by sending
Schmeer across the plate. He earned his
way to first by a hit. went to third on a
hit to center from Moski man's bat. and
scored on Harvey's fumble of a hot ground
ball which Borchers swatted. The score
was thus tied, and so it remained until
the end of the fifth.
Two runs were added to Sacramento
score in the sixth. Harvey went to first
on an error of Dunleavy and Schmeer
allowed O'Conner to emulate Harvey.
Then came Eagan, who punished the ball
for two bases, scoring Harvey and O'Con
nor. In this inning Dunleavy, who made
first on the forcing out of Donovan at
second, scored on Lance's deep center hit
for two bases.
In the seventh the Dudes caught Har
vey on the right spot. Four hits and
four runs were the net products of the
discovery of Harvey's curves. Hutchin
son was sent to first and scored immedi
ately after on Hammond's two-base hit.
Hardie whacked the ball out to center
and Hammond scored. Then came Dono
van for a single and 1 unleavy for a
double, which meant the scoring of Hardie
and Donovan, to the great chagrin of
Sacramento's pride. In the following
inning four more hits were worked off
Harvey, but this time they netted but
two runs, made by Borchera and Hutch
inson. t<
Borchers pitched a remarkably good
game, striking six men out and allowing
but four hits. The prettiest fielding work j
of the day was a sensational stop by
Schmeer, putting one runner out at sec
ond and doubling the man going to first.
The score:

Sacramento 3, Oakland 1.
At no time during the morning game at
Oakland were the dress suit case-packers
of. Broadway in the running. For seven
innings the wise politicians of the capital

in the eighth Lange crossed the plate for

cier.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, MONDAY, SEPTEMBER 25. 1899.
DANCED AND MADE MERRY AT THE
BUTCHERS' OUTING AND BARBECUE
Striking Features of the Benevolent Butchers' Picnic.
ACCORDING to one of the "swagger" set, the last hop
of the picnic season given in Sunset Park. Santa
Cruz Mountains, yesterday, was strictly au fait.
The occasion for the outpouring of southside soci
ety was the annual outing and barbecue of the Journey
men Butchers' Protective and Benevolent Association.
That it was a genuine success will be attested by nearly
a thousand merry-makers who participated in the day's
pleasures. Only one tight marred the festivities, but this
was so speedily fjuel'.ed by alert deputy sheriffs that few
dii the grounds were aware of it. One of the participators
in the brawl afterward admitted to his friends that a
"guy" tried to steal his girl and he simply emphasized
his right to monopolize her dance programme by "hand
ing" him one.
The barbecue proved a taking feature of the picnic.
Jovial Ben Davis, the lord high chef, and his humble as
sistants, F. C. Dana. Charles Rosenberg, Joe Williams
and "Harry" Bar, were on the scene the night before,
and whin the two sections of passenger cars rolled into
the picnic grounds at noon everything was in readiness for
the guests. Choice ribs and loins of beef had been prop
erly broiled, and pitchforks were used to remove the ap
petizing meat from the novel oven to the tables, to be

GOOD ENTRY LIST.
Louisville Driving and Fair Associa
tion Promise Good Sport.
LOUISVILLE. Sept. 24.â A1l entries are
completed for the fifth meeting of the
Louisville Driving and Fair Association,
which begins Monday and continues ar
week. There are four races each day,
and during the meeting $40,000 in purses
will be distributed. All of the crack trot
ters and pacers which have gone on the
grand circuit are now here. The principal
events of the meeting are the Kentucky
Matron stake, $7000, for three-year-old
trotters, in which there are seventeen en
tri.s; the 2:08 pace, for J2OOO, in which
Sally Toler (2:O6Vi). Indiana (2:07%), Hal
B (2:04Â»4), Lady of the Manor (2:04 1 4Â», Ar
lington (2:07) and Nieol B (2:08^) are en
tered. These two events are down for the
first day.
The Douglass stake, on Tuesday, for
i carved into sandwiches and dispensed to all who would
partake. The meat was so tempting that everybody ate
heartily, and the men in charge of the counter were kept
busy all the afternoon nerving the crowd.
Then was not a lull in the dancing. Throughout the
afternoon the waxed floor of the spacious pavilion was
crowded with devotees of the light fantastic, who glided,
bopped and pirouetted to such soulful and exhilarating
melodies as "She Is More to Be Pitied Than Chlded,"
"How I Like to See My Poor Old Father Work." "When
Dewey Comes Marching Home" and other popular songs.
R. C. Dunbar, who acted as floor manager, was given that
distinguished honor because he was the only member of
the association who was a member of the California vol
unteers. He was ably assisted by Frank Krauer, H. F.
Schwab, Ben Osswald, Morris Marks. Arthur O'Reilly
and J. J. Shepston. All the ladles were attired in light
outing dresses, which contrasted very nicely with the
vari-colored shirts of the men, who, mindful of their own
comfort, discarded their coats and vests.
At 5 o'clock the picnickers left the beautiful grounds,
and another pleasant event had passed into the memory
of those who attended.

to be one of the star events. Peter the
Great (2:07%), Sarah S (2:09). Charley Herr
(2:lu. Dr. L.cc (2Mis, Tudor Chimes (2:12) â ,

No Work Done on Sunday by Either
the Men or Officers.
NEJW YORK. Sept. 24.â The Shamrock
and her consort, the steam yacht Erin,
lay quietly at their anchorage within the
Horseshoe to-day, the Shamrock's crew
enjoying a full day of rest and bracing
themselves for the hard work before j
them. Sir Thomas also spent a quirt day.
devoting his time to his c<>rresi>ondenee
and to the entertainment of a few friends
who spent the day on board the Erin.
The big gaff, which was broken in one
of the tests of last week, has been re
paired and was shipped Saturday night
after tho Shamrock's return from her ex
traordinary performance of that day.
That spar will be in place when the chal
lenger goes out to-morrow fur another
test of speed. Weather favoring, the trial
will be a fifteen-mile beat to windward \
and return. Only a few more days re
main in which thf> Shamrock can be tested
and "tuned up" and. realizing that fact,
Sir Thomas has decided that no more op
portunities can be neglected and he means
to make the best use of the little time
that is left. The yacht will probably be
given a spin to-day and to-morrow, and
on Wednesday it is likely that she will be
placed in the drydock and the under
water section given a thorough cleaning.
After she come out of the dock she will
probably be tested every day. save Sun
day and the day on which she will be
measured, the date of which has not yet
been decided.

ON THE CRICKET FIELD.
Alamedas Defeat Pacifies in a Closely
Fought Match on the Home
Grounds.
The Alameda cricket team scored an
other victory yesterday over its old
rivals, the Pacifies. Captain George
Theohald of the Pacifies won the toss and
took the field. Though there was no
heavy batting on the Alameda side, six
of the batsmen made double figures, the
highest scorers being J. H. Saunders and
V. E. Seebeck, each of whom compiled
22 runs. Of the Pacific bowlers Coles
proved the most effective, taking 5
wickets for 26 runs; Casidy took 3 for 30
and Harbour 1 for 34.
The Pacifies were without the services
of one of their best batsmen, J. Myers,
but with 44 runs for 2 wickets down their
chances of victory looked by no means
bad. When 7 wickets had fallen for 79
runs the prospects of winning looked
slender. The last three wickets raised the
total to 107, and tnus the match ended in
a win for the Alamedas by 15 runs. The
Alamedas tried six bowlers, of whom
Fortmann took 3 wickets for 16 runs, Mc-
Ix-an 3 for 22 and Ward Jr. 2 for 26. The
highest scorers ior the losers were George
Theobald 20 and H. P. G. Gordon 18. In
the Pacific team there were three Theo
balds, George, his brother John and his
son Bert. The full details of the game
follow below:

LOS ANGELES TURF MEET
Los Angeles cannot complain of ill
treatment on the part of the California
Jockey Chin. The race meeting to be
held at the southern metropolis, October
21 to 28, will undoubtedly be a very suc
cessful one, and all owners of racing
horses desirous of racing: there will be
furnished free transportation upon appli
cation to J. C. Humphrey, the book
maker. Thomas H. Williams Jr. states
that horsemen journeying- there can have
stalls at Oakland track reserved until
tbeir return.
The races are conditioned to suit all
sorts and classes of horses, with two
harness and four running rac< s daily.
No entrance fee will be charged in over
night purses. The Los Angeles Derby â
with a cup and $500 added at one am! :i
half mile?â will hn the special runninng
feature, the entries for which close wiih
Secretary Thurm on October 9. A purse
is also offered for hurdle horses.
In the harness line, the great three
cornered contest between Joe Patchen,
.1. !.:: R. Gentry and Anaconda, for which
{5000 la hung up. will be the star attrac
tion. It has been agreed upon to split
the big stake, the trio meeting for one
half of it on Saturday, October 21. and
again coming together for the other por
tion on the Saturday following. It should
prove a tremendous drawing card.

BRISTOL. R. 1.. Sept. 24.â The cup de
fender Columbia left here this morning
for N'\v Rochelle, the home of ('. Oliver
Iselln. The crew was astir early, prepar

resnoffs to witness the departure of the

purpose of having her under body cleaned.

they would be open from noon till mid
night. This obviated the matter and
made it plain that there was at least one
break daily in the performance, which
made it legal for the collector to demand
one license fee each day. Should he have
the duty of taxing the municipal circus

The politicians are in grave danger.
The county has had forced upon it some
things of interest that are likely to in
terfere with the vanity of the job-chaser.
From Tesla to West Oakland everybody
is busy except the county's prisoners and
the willfully lazy. There is not, by 25 per
cent, enough labor in the county at this
time to do its work, whether in the can
nery or in the building of new houses and
business blocks. The newspapers have
taken on an encouraging tone and enjoy
the supreme satisfaction of . publishing
daily columns of information relating to
the scores of new homes and commercial
buildings that are being erected; the new
contracts for wharf building and for

reports of the fruit growers, canners and
dryers that show how good prices have

Now It is as bad in its operation as it is

to those fostering it, but who are only
responsible secondarily for BUCb condi
tions, the chief odium being with those
who made such conditions possible.
With a reconstructed Board of Works
the charter will give satisfaction, pro

sive administration and which shall not
be dependent upon the political whims of

Adna A. Dennison, the city editor of a
local paper, should have trouble enough
on his bands hereafter. He has given to
the world suggestions as to how to write
to and for newspapers. In his opening
paragraph he says: "In these days when
every citizen considers that he has an
inalienable right to write for the news
papers, and not only communications on
"DOMINIE" DhNNIf-ON'S HfcAVEN.
various topics, but matters of news are
contributed by persons not regularly at
tached to the staff, it ought to be a part
of the education of even' mdiv dual to
know how to properly prepare and ad
dress a communication to a newspaper.
It is one of the essential things which is
omitted from public school courses."
The rest of Mr. Dennison's article sets
forth in detail how to write, what to
write, whom to send to, and what to tell
the editor. Mr. Dennlson evidently takes
great pleasure in encouraging "every
citizen" to exercise hie "inalienable
right," and so that he may not be dis
couraged I would advise that the 70/KX)
citizens of Oakland exercise their right
by sending a sample communication on
any old thing to Mr. Dennison. His ad
dress is Adna A. Dennison, city editor,
Enc4uirer. Oakland.
A few weeks ago a celebrated San Fran
cisco writer came to Oakland and opened
a school for the diffusion of knowledge
relative to newspaper writing. Mr. Den
nison. who is an ard< tit advocate of
home trading, evidently believes that it is
Quite postble for local talent to supply
all the needed lore of this nature in this
city.
Although a year away, the fight over
the Supervlsorship is already waxinÂ«
warm. Next year three Supervisors will
retire. This is a majority of the board.
The retiring members are Tom Wells of
Murray Township, Charles Ro.-th and
John Mitchell of Oakland. T'nless some
thing unforeseen happens, it is hardly
likely that another Democrat will suc
ceed Mr. Wells. Thre.- years ago there
was a bitter factional fight between the
Republicans of Murray and Washington
Township, which was carried beyond the
convention to the polls and the W;ushing
ton Republicans voted with the Demo
erats. electing Wells. Now party lines
are better harmonized, and it is- believed
that a Washington man will win the Re
publican nomination, especially as Mur
ray has received several appointments in
the county government during the past
two years. Ex-Supervisor Hiram Bailey
and Assemblyman William McDonald are
already laying wires, and there are about
a dozen others.
In Oakland John Mitchell and Charles
Roeth will have no walk-over, although
each is anxiotis to succeed himself.
Mitchell has been guilty of the great po
litical mistake of not standing steadily
by any one faction. He has passed the
hat around and has landed where he
cannot with any great claim demand the
support of any particular element in his
party. Unlike Church and his class of
politicians who always stand by their
friends through thick and thin and come
up smiling after the election, Mitchell
has temporized and at times tins at
tempted to carry water on both shoulders
until he has become a much weaker man
toward the end of his term than he was
at the beginning.
Dr. Pierce, the veterinary surgeon, and
W. S. Wixson of North Oakland would
like to be Supervisors and already they
are marshaling their friends in their be
half. There are half a dozen other candi
dates anxious to step into Mr. Mitchell's
shoes.
Charles Roeth has been cleverly repair
ing his broken ferces during the past few
months, and has regained some of the
ground he hae" lost a year ago. But there
are many anxious for his scalp and it is
already evidenced that he will have no
easy task to succeed himself.

SCHMIDT DREW A GUN.
Fruitvale Man Runs His Fath-?r-in-
Law Into the Street.
OAKLAND, Sept. 24.â The bitter feeling
existing for some time between Henry
Miner, residing in the Allendale Tract at
Fruitvale, and his son-in-law, J. G.
Schmidt, reached its climax to-night Ah
a result the son-in-law was arrested by
Deputy Sheriff Qulnlan on a charge of
assault with a deadly weapon.
Schmidt and his wife have been sepa
rated for some time. This evening he
called on her at the home of her father,
Mr. Miner, when the latter, so Schmidt
claims, accused him of having purloined
a watch. After a heated argument
Schmidt is said to have whipped out a re
volver, fully loaded, and threatened to
shoot off the top of his father-in-law's
head. Miner rushed from his home and
sought protection at the hands of Deputy
Sheriff Quinlan. Schmidt showed tight
when the officer attempted to place him
under arrest. A desperate struggle fol
lowed, but the officer proved quite equal
to the occasion, felling his man and haul
-Ins him to the County Jail.

Electric Car Wrecks a Buggy.
OAKLAND, Sept. 24.â A San Pablo ave
nue electric car crashed into a horse and
buggy to-night at Fourth street and
Broadway, demolishing the rig and injur
ing the horse so severely that it will have
to be shot. The driver, Harry Wells, aped
24 years, residing on Hancock street, as
poon as he realized what had happened,
ran away, leaving the wreck for others to
attend. The horse and busgy are the
property of Thomas McKenzie, a livery
man at Thirteenth and Center streets,
who says Wells ordered the rig by tele
phone this morning, representing himself
as Williams, a produce merchant.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, SEPTEMBER 25, 1899.
JUDGE STAN LY'S
REMAINS LAID
TO FINAL BEST
His Late Employes as
Pallbearers.
â  Â«
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 24.
The funeral of Judge John A. Stanly,
who died at his home, 1221 Jackson street,
this city, last Friday morning, Uok place
this afternoon at St. Paul's Episcopal
Church, of which the deceased was one
of the founders. The services, consisting
of the usual Episcopalian ritual, were Im
pressively conducted by Rev. Dr. Robert
Ritchie, rector of the church, and Rev.
John Bakewell. rector of Trinity Church.
St. Paul's was thronged with many
friends of the departed Jurist, and the
floral tributes were numerous and appro
priate, the more noticeable pieces being
broken wheels, broken columns and sev
eral beautiful pillows and crosses. There
was no music save an organ dirge.
The casket was borne by employes of
the deceased, fifteen of whom came down
from his ranch near Napa to bid a last
farewell to their departed employer.
The honorary pallbearers were Judge E.
W. McKinstry, Judge John Garber, C. D.
Allen, Warren Olney, Harry W. Bradley,
George H. Wheaton, Shatter Howard and
Henry Rogers.
The services at the grave in Mountain
View Cemetery were simple, and only the
family, immediate relatives and the pall
bearers, in accordance with an expressed
wish of the deceased, accompanied the
remains to the burial grounds.

Sparks From a Chimney Cause De
struction of a Laundry.
OAKLAND, Sept. 24.â French laun
dry on Haywards avenue near Estudillo
street, San Leandro, owned by M. Gautier,
was entirely destroyed by lire at an early
hour this morning. The cause of the
blaze is not known, but it is presumed .o
have started from sparks from the fur
nace chimney. Gautier and his wife were
working in the lower story of the build-
Ing and did not discover the flames until
the entire upper portion of the building
was ablaze. Much of the contents was
saved. The proprietor lost ah ox con
taining $'.Â«) in coin and his wife had jew
elry valued at $300 consumed by the fire.
M Causse, brother-in-law of Gautier.
was in bed when the fire broke out and
barely escaped in his night clothes from
the burning building. Two pet dogs that
were in the building were burned to death.
The loss la estimated at $2000 and is cov
erd by an insurance of $1000. The Fire De
partment succeeded in saving the adjoin
ing buildings.

DEATH OF A PIONEER RESIDENT
Henry Schuler, an Early Settler in
This State, Passes Away at
Alameda.
ALAMEDA. Sept. 24.â Henry Schuler, a
native of Germany, aged 12 years, died at
g;S6 i.clock this morning at his residence,
531 Central avenue, after a lingering ill
ness.
Mr. Schuler was a pioneer of this State,
having come here from Ohio nearly fifty
years ago and settled in the mines in
Tuolumne County. For a good many
y. ars he has been a resident of this city
and at one time took considerable Interest
in municipal affairs. He was the pro
prietor of the Palace Brewery at the
west Kii'l. and up to the time of his last
Illness had taken an active interest in the
business. He was a widower and the
father of the late John M. and Lawrence
L. Schuler and -Mrs. 15. tiulse. He was
a member of Columbia Lodge No. 8, !â¢'.
and A. M., of Tuolumne County.

Union Revival Meetings.
OAKLAND, Sept. 24.â A season of union
revival meetings will be inaugurated in
the First Methodist Episcopal Church on
Wednesday evening. October 4, by Rev. J.
H. Weber, the evangelist, who has had
very signal success recently in Sacra
mento, Santa Cruz, Grass Valley, Napa
and Eureka. The meetings here will be
participated in by all the Methodist i
Episcopal churches in the four sections I
of this city. After about one week In the I
First Church the meetings will be trans
ferred to one of the other churches.

DUNGEONS USUALLY
BREED SKEPTICISM
Rev. William Rader of the Third Con
gregational Church delivered a sermon
last nigtit on "From Desert tv Dungeon."
In part he said:
John was a sensational preacher. He was
original In dress, being clothed with sklna;
original In what he ate and what he said. To
httn belongs the honor of presenting Jesus to
the world and of baptizing 'him In the Jordan, j
This was a greater honor than writing an j
epic or smashing a fleet. He came in contact '
with Herod, whose domestic life he attacked. !
The rustic preacher retained the iron of his ;
logic and the strength of his purpose before
the throne. The voluptuous life ot the court j
did not injure him. But he was cast Into I
prison.
The open air has done much for faith. It
Is easier to believe under God's great sky
than In a dungeon. Dungeons usually breed
Bkepticlsm. They crush courage. John the
Baptist doubted and he sent to Christ for a
vindication of his faith. The answer was that
people were being healed and helped and
saved.
Civilization, good laws, good lives, good,
charity, virtue and benevolence is the bp*t
answer to the appeal fr"m the dungeons.
Great lives are better than pood books.

SAN FRANCISCO, ;CAl*;';:*i
and bits of personal experience, which add
largely to their interest.
His sermon in the morning was on the
"Keynote of Victory." and was taken
from the text, "Have Faith in God. He
explained the wonderful power of faith,
the prayer of which was always answered
by divine Providence, and that it was by
faith more than by words and works that
victory over the evil one was achieved.
His subject In the evening related to the
necessity of tho co-operation of the indi
vidual in bringing souls to the kingdom
of God, which was the true mission of the
church.
"This mission can be successful in va
rious ways," he said. "One of these is to
go to men with love in our hearts and talk
to them about their souls. Men could be
brought to salvation by earnest personal
effort, by our living a blameless life,
whereby they would be convinced that
there Is reality in the Christian religion.
Again, a life of Christian Joy is a splendid
way to reach lost souls.
"T do not believe in long-faced Chris
tianity," concluded Dr. Bane. "It is sinful
for a Christian to be gloomy. He ought
to be happy, both in his life and in his
testimony. Live a life of self-sacrifice, of
tenderness and love, and many will be en
couraged to come into the kingdom of
God."
DR. WILSON DWELLS
ON PIONEER DAYS
AKGONATTTS IN A BODY HEAR
HIS SERMON.
More Than a Hundred Members of
the California Society Listen to
Pleasant Reminiscences.
Following in the footsteps of ex-Judge
Niles Searls, 150 members of the Society
of California Pioneers marched into the
Howard Methodist Church yesterday fore
noon to hear pleasant reminiscences of the
days when there were no electric lights
shining on the streets of San Francisco.
In his discourse Rev. Dr. John A. B. Wil
son said in part:
Napoleon paid that he who made two
blades of grass to grow where but one
grew before was a benefactor of the race.
The principlf BO illustrated Is pound. We
are in the world to make things better,
not only to consume and absorb but propa
gate and develop.
I am glad to have with us tn-dny and
to welcome the men who laid the founda
tion of this empire upon the Pacific. It
is destined to become the greatest State in
the I'nlon. In tifty years its population
and wraith will be greater than New York,
and the cities of San Francisco Bay will
excel in size and grandeur the Greater
New York of to-day. But following the
divinely implanted ambition and enter
prise of your young manhood ye laid the
foundations of civilization upon which it
nil must he builded. The text which oc
curs to me a? most suitable for this occa
sion I read from psalm lxxxiv:6, "Who
passing thmugh the valley of Haca make it
a well the rain also filling the pools."
It 1b' our allotment in life to o ten pass
through arid places. It "is a privilege of
life to make the arid wastes better for
our passage over them. It belongs also
to life to enjoy the wells )f our own dig
ging and leave them as a better condition
of life for the betterment "f others.

fW FAVORITE
FAVORITE
SCRIPTION
FOR WEAK WOMEN. ;
The world is slow to learn that the
heroes of peace are not less worthy of
honor than those of war. Yours have
been the triumphs of peace. The soldier
is glorified while the llfesaver upon a dan
gerous coast is forgotten. But as civili
zation advances we shall learn more and
more that it takes the same sterling and
manly qualities to win peaceful triumphs
as those of war.
1.-et us not forget that the duties and
relationships of the present are analogous
to the higher ilfe of the soul. We cannot
remain here, we are going hence, but we
i want to go hence in pence. Without im
mortality there is no climax to life, no
ultimatum to existence. We are going to
live forever somewhere. We older people
are approaching a new birth to an Im
mortal life. Out from tht school of the
present to the great university of God"s
upper and other worlds we shall begin
all anew with the furnishlnss of the pres
ent estate for our capital. But some
things are open to the aged. We can en
list In the armies of the heavenly king
dom and to battle for a better condition
of life, for the uplift of the humanity about
us. Gentlemen, before we go hence, let
us make sure for ourselves of Tightness
with God. and be sure that an Influence
emanates from us that has helpfulness in
It for others. He who loves God best loves
his fellow best: there Is no way to demon
strate fealty to God but by helpfulness to
man. He lives for God the most who
lives for humanity the best.

FATHER WYMAN SPEAKS
ON OUR LADY OF MERCY
At St. Mary's Church last evening
Father Wyman preached on the . feast
celebrated yesterday by the Catholic
churchâ that of Our Lady of Mercy. He
said:
The blessed virgin Is distinguished more
by the attribute of mercy than by any
other. There is no title by which Chris
i tians delight to address her more than
- that of "Mother of Mercy." She was
raised up by the Almighty to be the In
strument of mercy to all mankind. God
has never made or formed but one enmity
; and that is an Irreconci liable one. It is
that which exists between Mary, his
worthy mother, and Satan. In all the con
flicts between Satan and mankind the
blessed virgin has been the one who in
accordance with the prophecy of man's
redemption, has crushed the head of the
serpent.

his love before he can marry you. Hn\t
does he expect him to prove it?" "By
figures, I imagine." â Brooklyn Life.

. PROPOSALS.
BAN FRANCISCO, Cal., August 29 IS99â Seal-
ed proposals, in triplicate, will be received
here until 12 o'clock m., September 28, . 1599,
and then opened, for furnishing coal for Pre-
sidio of San Francisco, Cal., during fiscal
year' ending June 30. 1900. Government re-
serves right to. reject or accept any and all
bids or any part thereof. Preference given
to articles of domestic production, conditions
of quality and price (Including In the price
of foreign productions the duty thereon) being
equal, and such preference given to articles
of American production produced on Pacific
Coast to extent of consumption required by
the public service there. _ Information fur-
nished on application to undersigned. En-
velopes containing proposals : should be in-
dorsed "Proposals for coal," and addressed
to I M. Marshall, '. Deputy Quartermaster
General, U. S. A., Chief Quartermaster.
PROPOSALS will ' be received at â¢ the Bureau
of Supplies and Accounts. Navy -Department,
Washington. D. C, until 12 o'clock noon,
October 3, 1899. and publicly opened Immedi-
ately thereafter, to furnish at the Navy Yard.
Mare Island, Cal., one locomotive crane, also
a quantity of steel shapes, steel plates,
broken stone and ' lumber. Blank proposals
will be furnished upon application to the
Bureau" or to the Navy Pay Office. . San,
Francisco. Cal. A. S. KENNY, . Paymaster j
Central IT, S. N. 9-5-99. i

Uncle Bill's Ideas.
The greater a man's temper becomes the
easier It is to lose it.
A woman may be a poor marksman,
but she generally knows how to throw a
hint.
The young man with the money to
burn generally loses what glowing pros
pects he may have had.
Some men who were the architects of
their own fortunes would never have had
one if they hadn't built on sand.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The area, of high pressure central off Van
couver Island last evening has movod inland
and Is central over the Rocky Mountain region
this evening. The pressure is lowest In South
ern California and Southern Arizona. It is
above the normal In all districts.
The temperature has risen slightly in all dis
tricts except nlong the central coast of Califor
nia, where there haa lieen a fall. The greatest
rise has been over Northern Nevada and South
ern Idaho. There haa also been a decided rise
over Northwestern Oregon.
The weather is clear in all districts except
foggy along the Central California coast.
The temperature generally exceeded 100 de
grees In the Interior valley of California,
Conditions are favorable for fair and con
tinued warm weather In California except
foggy along the coast of Central California at
Forecast made Rt Ran Francisco for thirty
hours, ending midnight. September 24. 1899:
Northern Californiaâ Fair Monday, except
foggy along the central coast at night; light
northwest wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Monday; light west
wind.
Nevada â Fair Monday.
Utahâ Fair; warmer Monday .
Arizonaâ Fair Monday.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Monday, ex
cept foggy in the morning; fresh west wind.
G. H. WILLSON,
Local Forecast Official.

MONDAY, SEPTEMBER 25.
NOTE â In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column glveÃ the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides as sometimes occur. The heights
given are in addition to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low water 3.

PLANS FOR THE
FORMATION OF A
GREAT LIBRARY
â¦
Union of Mechanics
and Mercantile.
Â»
A MUCH-TALKED-OF SCHEME!
â¦
PROBABLY PERMANENT CLOS- ,
ING OF THE FAIR
It Has Not Been ProfitableâPropo
sition to Sell the Real Estate
and Reorganize the Insti
tution.
*-,
There will be a peerless library in San
Francisco before a year is out if the
plans now being talked of among the
members of the Mechanics' Institute and
the Mercantile Library be carried to a j
satisfactory conclusion. A union of the
two Institutions is sought by many of the -
members of both, and the idea is gaining
in favor the more it cornea under dlscus
eion. It takes prominence too from the
fact that the Mechanics' Library is in a
state of transition, and it will not be long
before there is a definite change in its
financial policy. For years It has been
looked upon by a great portion of its ,
membership as a money making institu
tion, but it has come to be believed that i
to keep abreast of the times the institu- :
tion must devote more to the library
itself and less to the holding of property j
and the giving of fairs, and so it may be
that the- exposition now in progress at the
old pavilion will be the last the people of j
this city can attend in a long time.
The plan of uniting the two libraries has â 
been spoken of among the members for
some months, but there has been no defi
nite action taken by any one in authority.
It has become too important a subject to
be passed without notice, however, and
already the idea of calling a conference
between the officers of the two libraries is
taking shape. It is expected this meeting
will take place soon after the closing of
the fair, and the advocates of the scheme \
are sure the conference can end in but ;
one way â a union of the two and the
formation of the biggest and finest sub
scription library in the United States.
For months past the finances of the
Mercantile Library have been in a most
unquiet condition. There is a mortgage !
of JTo.iOO on the property on the corner of
Golden Gate and Van Ness avenues, and.
It takes all thÂ»; income from the dues and :
the rental of the building to pay the in- j
terest on this and to keep the Institution :
going. It has not been progressing in any
way and a corporation of that kind can
not remain stationary. The library must
be maintained up to date, and the prop
erty kept in repair, and the income of the
institution is nardly sufficient to do all
this. Something must be done, and tne
members have been looking around for
some way out of the difficulty. Various
means have been resorted to. but they
have either failed or have been laid aside,
and now the last step is being urged.
Either the .Mercantile must consolidate
with the Mechanics', or it cannot hope to '
run much i<Â«iiUtr on the basis that at
present exists. i
There has never been any doubt of the ;
excellence of the Mercantile Library. It
has elotfe to sixty thousand volumes, ln
ciuding a reference library that could
hardly be equaled. It would be a real ad
cition to tne Mechanics' Institute, for it
has upon its shelves thousands of volumes
not to be found in '::â¢â¢ Mechanics' Library
nor anywhere else, it would be a sham*
to scatter the collection, and there is no'
one here whose literary tastes and whose
pocket-book are bo thoroughly in accord i
as to enaule him to purchase the library ;
in its entirety. As a consequence, the
idea of consolidation is looked upon with j
the greatest favor oy the members of the !
Mechanics' and particularly by those
who are members of both institutions.
There are me hundreds of these, and :
among them are the warmest supporters
of the plan. There is need of change in
the downtown institution as well as in
the other, and the union with the Mer
cantile is looked upon as a good start to
ward greater prosperity. '
Ine auoiiuoa vi tile fair is another
matter receiving the attention of the dis- ;
cursive, for i..c exposition has not been
paying much In late years* and this season
it looks as if there would be a heavy
deficit to face at the close of the exmoi
tion. The exposition has not been able to
pay without the methods of the experi
enced showmen, anu the officers of the in
stitute declare they are not showmen
and if the exposition cannot be made to
pay otherwise it la better to stop the an
â¢ nual enterprise.
There is more in contemplation than
this, however, for with the closing for
good of the fair the property must be
sold, and as soon as the Larkin-Btreet
property Is disposed of the Institute will
make other changes that have been
aiscussed for some i,me. it is proposed
to move from the present location on
Post street and establish the library on
some downtown corner, selling tne old
â  place and if possible the property on
Folsom and Sixteenth streets.
. There has always been opposition to tne
selling of any of the property of the in
stitution, ana to prevent any such course
a resolution was passed a year or so ago
-forbidding the sale of any real property
by the board of directors. Conditions
have changed nee then, however, and it
Would not now be a difficult matter to se
cure the consent of a majority of : the
members for such a sale, and if the fairs
are stopped a sale of the property must
follow.
It is figured that from the sale of the
Post street realty and the Larkln street
property enough could be realized to pur
chase a new site in some favorable loca
tion, erect a suitable building and leave
on hand a capital of nearly $400,000 with
which to run th.- library. This might be
made to yield an income of $20,000 a year,
a sum sufficient, with the other sources of
income, to keep the institution in com
parative plenty.
Should the consolidation scheme go
through the result would be a library of
over 100,000 volumes, and one of almost
inestimable value. The merging of the
two memberships would make a strong
following, and the influence of the insti
tution would be more than doubled in
stead of. as now. being divided.
The matter will be decided one way or
the other soon after the fair closes. As
yet the heads of the two institutions will
not discuss the subject officially, but mere
is no active opposition to the plan, and
there is every Indication that it will be
carried to a successful conclusion.
TRIUMPHANT PROGRESS
OF CHRISTIANITY
Rev. E. Nelander of the First English
Lutheran Church delivered a sermon last
night on "The Triumphant Progress of
Christianity." In part he said:
Â£v*At the beginning of this century the esti
mated population of the world was placed
at 1,000,000.000. To-day it is put at M 40.000
-.-000. Then the estimated number of Chris
tian adherents was 200.000,000. Mow it is
â¢477,000.000. During the century the per cent
Increase in population has been about 44.
The per cent increase In the number of
Christian* for the same period has been
more than 135. In the year 1800 there were
about 141.000,000 under Christian government.
Since then the number has been constantly
Increasing until to-day it â  has reached
891,000,000. There are a million and a half
of converts to itaOW for this century of work
among the heathen. Since we gathered here
last Sabbath 2000 souls, at the lowest esti
mate, have been brought into the light of
Christianity, and that among Pagan nations
only. And who shall lift the veil of the
next century?
We may not generalize amid the smoke of
battle but the backward look makes one
thing 'clear: Everything is going right. Let
111 to the /watch-tower: the King's banners
are waving on the distant hills.
Fell Down the Stairs.
Henry Schumacher, steward in the
Creajaerie, on Market street, near Fifth,
slipped on the stairs leading to the kitchen
yesterday afternoon and fell to the bot
tom. He was taken to the Receiving Hos
ptial, where Dr. Rice found that his left
shoulder and wrist had been fractured.

The transport Valencia arrived in port
yesterday morning, bringing from Manila j

Volunteers and members of various other !
regiments. The Montanans predominated,]
and furnished another day of rejoic
ing for the reception committee from their
State. So early did the transport arrive
that the reception committee was taken
by surprise, but the members soon were
afloat In two steamers, and the eitiz' -
reception committee of San Francisco j
soon appeared in another gayly decorated
steamer to add the cordial welcome of a
sister State.
There were 437 Montana officers and sol
diers on the Valencia fresh from the Phil
ippines. Of these twenty-six were in
valids, who were- in various stages of con
valescence. The 400 and odd other war
riors were decidedly able-bodied, tanned
the color of leather, keyed up to the pitch
of Bhoutiog whenever and wherever they
sajv a Montana face beaming fondly upon |
them from a steamer's deck; whenever
and wherever they saw a Montana hand
extended to them in welcome.
They shunted, cheered, iaughed and
half-cried, and the commit tie of recep- I
lion from the Mountain State way equally
affected. The lirst Installment of the re
ceiving Montanans boarded the customs j

cia, having been inspected and about to

phens, mother of Serjeant Stephens; Mrs.

San Franciscans.
The Valencia was under way before the
: Gate was fairly turned. So there
was a stern chase', but the Golden Gate
soon overhauled the transport and threw
out lines and a gangplank, and a reunion
of Montana soldiers and their friends fol
io w( < i .
Hardly had the Valencia dropped an
chor when the second detachment of the
reception committee arrived on the Gov
ernor Markham. The central group there
was made up of Governor Smith of Mon
tana and staff.
"What's the matter with Governor
Smith?" shouted the Boldiers as they saw
the ( rovernor.
"He's all rfcin, you bet."
With the Governor was Mrs. Smith, Ad
iutant General English. Colonels Firch.
McTeague and J. T. Stanford. Mr. Thomp

After the welcomes had all heen extended

So far no camp regulations have been

Pay from the beginning of July i.^ due

THE SAN FKAXCISCO CALL, MONDAY, SEPTEMBER 25. 139f1.
BROTHER OF PRESIDENT
McKINLEY IN THE CITY
ABNER MeKINLEY, brother of the President, arrived in the city yes
terday morning and Is now at the Palace. He came from New York,
staying- a day en route at Chicago, Omaha, Salt Lake and Denver, and '
â¢will leave Tuesday evening for the southern part of the State to visit <
Loa Angeles and Coronado. With him are his wife and daughter and the .
balance of the party consists of Major R. C. Smith and William Endsley of <
Philadelphia and Colonel William H. Brown of New York. ,
Yesterday Mr. McKinley was taken in charge by his nephew, George
E. Morse, clerk of the United States District Court, and after spending the *
afternoon in driving about the city was entertained by him at dinner. '
Mr. McKinley, who bears a striking resemblance to his famous brother, <
was seen yesterday, but absolutely refused to say anything of a political na- .
ture for publication.
"I am no politician," said he. "All my time Js taken up with my law prac- '
tice In New York and I have no leisure to devote to anything demanding '
so much attention as politics. This is the tlrst visit I have made to the coast <
since 73, when I came out here to visit my brothers. James and David, who ,
are now dead, but who were then engaged in business hen-. I find the city not (
only greatly changed, but wonderfully Improved as well, and when I look
about me 1 can easily understand the reason the people of San Francisco <
take so much pride in their home. .
I will remain here until Tuesday evening, when I leave for Coronado and <
Los Angeles, where I will spend a few days and then return to this city,
leaving almost Immediately for my home, via Portland and the northern '
route. 1 was obliged to attend to some private Business matters of my own <
in the West and so took the opportunity thus afforded to get a little rest Â«
and visit my relatives on the coast.
While here Mr. McKinley will attend the burial of his nephew. Joseph
Morse, the young lieutenant of the Ninth Infantry, who died of typhoid
fever in Manila, and whose body was shipped home to his parents at 1578 Fell '
street, from which place the funeral will take place next Tuesday. Â«

The Twenty-Sixth Leaves.
The Twenty-sixth Regiment will get
away to-day on the Grant. It was not
expected the command would spend so
little time at the Presidio, but the misfor- j
tunes of the Thirty-first has hastened its
departure.
The Grant came over from quarantine
Saturday night and anchored off the
Government dock. She was berthed yes
terday. This morning the first of the reg
iment's baggage will be put aboard and
before night it is hoped the transport will
be ready for sea.
All the baggage of the Thirty-first Reg
imi nt which was aboard wjis put off ;it
the detention camp on the island, the men i
r>f the regiment acting as stevedores and,
what was better, receiving stevedore*' j
pay for the work. The men worked with
a will and the transport was discharged
in the shortest possible time. Then the!
vessel was fumigated.
RAN ON A REEF
HID LAY THERE
SEVERAL HOURS
â¦
Leelanaw Had a Close
Call at Sea.
--âÂ«. â
ARRIVES IN PORT LEAKING
HER CABGO OF HORSES LEFT
AT HONOLULU.
Detailed Story of the Accident as It
Was Told by Captain Storrs,
Commander of the
Transport.
â¦
The United States transport Leelanaw,
that arrived yesterday from Honolulu,
narrowly escaped total wreck Saturday
on a reef south of Montara. She came
into port leaking and at once transferred
the cargo in her hold to the transport
Rio Janeiro, preparatory to going upon
the drydock for inspection and repairs.
She was ashore several hours on the
rocks, not far from a point where several
vessels have been totally wrecked. The
sea was luckily smooth and only a small
swell was stirring from the time she
struck to the time when she was worked
off into deep water. She is not badly in
jured. Yesterday as she lay beside the
Rio, shifting her cargo, she was able to
keep out the water with her donkey
pump. The story of the wreck is told by
Captain Storrs, who commands the .Lee
lanaw.
"The vessel grounded on a reef to the
south of Montara," he said, "about six
teen miles from the bar, during a fog so
thick that 1 could not sue the lookout. We
sailed from Honolulu September U and
had fine weather until the 20th. I was un
able to get a perfect observation on the
21st and got none on the 22d or 23d and
was compelled to run by reckoning. On
the 23d there was dense fog with clearing
intervals, but no chance for an observa
tion. By reckoning 1 ought to have been
at the South Farallon at 1 p. m. on the
23d. I had the patent lead going every
half hour from 11:30 a. m. to 12:30 p. m.
From then on the lead was going every
lifteen minutes until we got bottom in
86 fathoms at M:4.'> p. m. Then, after we
got the bottom, the lead was kept going
as fast as it could be worked.
"1 heard a fog whistle at 4:40 p. m.,
which was on the port bow. The patent
log was hauled in, and 1 found that one
blade of the rotator was missing. The
blasts of the, fog signal were very irregu
lar. I could' not make the intervals be
tween the blasts agree. At 4:45 o'clock
the lead showed thirty-five fathoms. We
slowed down to a dead slow to identify
the signal, the sound of which was indis
tinct and faint. The first officer, at 5:02
o'clock, showed ten fathoms. Before the
headway of the vessel could be checked
she grounded lightly on the reef.
"The soundings that were then taken
all around the ship showed nothing less
than 14 feet at the bow forward, deep
ening' quickly after passing one-third of
the length from forward. There were nine
fathoms under the stern. I gave orders to
j>ump out No. 1 ballast tank and to 11 11
Nos. 3 and 4, which were empty. The en
gines were all the time backing strongly,
out the tide was falling and we could noL
move her. The engines were slowed just
to hold her from going further on with
the small swell that was running. Every
thing was made ready to try again after
the tide made. Until 11:*) the snip rested
easy and then slewed around about four
points. I put the helm over to port and
ordered full speed astern. After roiling
and thumping several times she came off
in deep water. The water was coming
in the No. 1 ballast tank, but there was
no trouble in keeping it, free. We came
up off the bar. but couldn't find the light
snip, because it had been removed for re
palrs."
The reasons that Captain Storrs gives
for the accident are: "Lack of opportuni
ty fur observations to verify my position;
losing a blade off the rotator of the pat
ent !ng. which altered its rate and thereby
fouled my dead reckoning; running with
the wind and sea abeam for three days
set the ship further to the southeast than
1 allowed for."
The Leelanaw sailed from San Fran
cisco September 1 for Manila via Hono
lulu. She had <m board a cargo of horses
and a large amouni of commissary stores,
principally canned goods. On the trip
down to flonoiulu five horses died. When
the steamer reached Honolulu the horses
were put ashore for their health. There
they very soon developed a disease that
became epidemic and it was impracticable
tn take them on board again to continue

MEN ORDERED TO STRIKE.
Boilermakers Decide to Take the Long
Threatened Action.
The boilermakcrs" strike la on. A meet-
Ing of the men hold yesterday afternoon at
A. o. F. hall, 10" O'Farrell street. James
Ryan presiding, and after discussing the i
situation decided to Issue orders that all
boilermakers working f n r firms doing
work on United States-- army transports
Stay out until the ei^ht-hour system is
acknowledged. It was also decided that I
any boilennaker who works on any j
transport until the eight-hour rule is [
adopted will be declared a "scab" for ;
life.
Chairman Ryan announced that the ;
meeting had been called for the purpose
of taking action on the recent orders of
the Pulton and Risdon Iron Works, which
Insisted that nine hours' work and ten j
hours' pay should constitute a day's work
on all contracts on United States trans
ports.
The boilermakers in the Fulton and the
Risdon shops, it was stated, are working
now inside ten hours at 32V,. cents per j
hour, while on outside jobs they work
nine hours for ten hours' pay. but they
refuse to work on transport contracts at
that rate, demanding $3 25 for a day's j
work of eight hours. These shops paid i
their men $;{ 25 for eight hours' work for
only one week, then insisting on the old :
rate, to which the boilermakers would not
agrees
The men claim that Captain Barneson,
superintendent of the transport, service,
had agreed with them that on transport
contracts eight hours would be considered
a day's work, and hail so declared him
self to a committee of boilermakers. The
EliSdon people insist that the committee |
had misunderstood ih< captain's meaning,
an'. 1 , acting on the suggestion of the com- [
mittee. the captain was called on and he
declared himself, in favor of the eight
hour system.
The boilermakers. despairing of making
a satisfactory settlement with the shops,
took action s : tated to order a strike.
Boilermakers were at work yesterday on
the Relief and the Marlposa, but they
will be called off to-day.
